Predictable arguments. Faonio et al. [\[FNV17\]](#page-43-5) previously showed that a predictable argument of knowledge for an NP language L implies an extractable witness encryption scheme for L. In the same work, they also note that the same construction can be used to obtain a standard (non-extractable) witness encryption scheme for L from any predictable argument for L. Here, a predictable argument is one where on each round, there is a single message that causes the verifier to accept, and moreover, this message is known to the verifier a priori. In our setting, we focus on argument systems where the prover can only send a single group element, so it suffices to just consider 2-message arguments. While Faonio et al. focused on the setting where the predictable argument satisfies perfect completeness (and negligible soundness), the same transformation still applies even if the scheme has a large completeness error (specifically, it suffices that 1 − c = 1/poly(λ), where c is the completeness error). We provide the definitions and main theorem statements below.

<span id="page-31-1"></span>Definition 5.3 (Predictable Laconic Argument [\[FNV17,](#page-43-5) adapted]). A 2-message laconic argument ΠLA = (QLA,PLA, VLA) is predictable if VLA(st, π) outputs 1 if and only if st = π. Namely, on input the security parameter λ and an instance x, the query algorithm QLA samples a query q together with a target proof string π, which is set as the verifier's state.

<span id="page-31-0"></span>Theorem 5.4 (Predictable Arguments to Witness Encryption [\[FNV17,](#page-43-5) adapted]). Let ΠLA = (QLA,PLA, VLA) be a 2-message predictable argument for an NP language L with completeness error c and soundness error ε = negl(λ). Then, there exists a witness encryption scheme for L with correctness error c/2.

We give a proof of Theorem [5.4](#page-31-0) in Appendix [E.](#page-66-0) The proof follows the structure of the corresponding proof in [\[FNV17\]](#page-43-5), just extended to the setting of imperfect completeness.

{32}------------------------------------------------

Arguments with generic verification. In the following, we will focus exclusively on arguments with a "generic" verification procedure. We say that a 2-message argument has a generic verification algorithm if the prover's message consists of a tuple of group elements, and the verifier's decision procedure only operates on the group elements in a generic manner (i.e., the verifier can only evaluate the group operation and test if two group elements are equal). We also allow the verifier to compute an arbitrary function (modeled as a Boolean circuit) of the results of the equality checks it performs on the group elements. For example, this model captures the verification algorithm of our ElGamal-based SNARGs from Section 3.2 (and more generally, any instantiation of the [BCI<sup>+</sup>13] compiler using ElGamal encryption). In fact, all of the group-based arguments we develop in this work have a generic verification procedure under our definition.

A similar notion of argument systems with a generic verification procedure was considered in [Gro16] in the pairing-based setting. The [Gro16] model was more restrictive in that it required that the verifier accept only if *all* of the (pairing-based) relations in the decision algorithm were satisfied. In our setting, we allow the verifier's decision to be any efficiently-computable function of the equality checks.

<span id="page-32-1"></span>**Definition 5.5** (Laconic Arguments with Generic Verification). Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}})$  be a 2-message laconic argument over a group  $\mathbb{G}$  of prime-order p with generator g. We say that  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}}$  has a generic verification algorithm if the following properties hold:

- The state st output by  $\mathcal{Q}_{LA}$  consists of the description of the group  $(\mathbb{G}, p, g)$ , a collection of nonzero vectors  $\mathbf{0} \neq \mathbf{a}_i \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$ , scalars  $b_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$  for  $i \in [m]$ , and the description of a Boolean circuit  $C : \{0, 1\}^m \to \{0, 1\}$ . Note that the components  $\{\mathbf{a}_i, b_i\}_{i \in [m]}$  as well as the circuit C can depend on the statement x.
- The proof output by  $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  is a vector of group elements  $g^{\boldsymbol{\pi}} \in \mathbb{G}^k$  where  $g^{\boldsymbol{\pi}} = (g^{\pi_1}, \dots, g^{\pi_k})$ .
- The verification algorithm  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  proceeds in two stages. On input a proof  $g^{\pi} \in \mathbb{G}^k$  and the verification state  $\mathsf{st} = (\{\mathbf{a}_i, b_i\}_{i \in [m]}, C)$ , the verification algorithm sets  $\beta_i = 1$  if  $g^{\mathbf{a}_i^{\mathsf{T}}\pi} = g^{b_i}$  and  $\beta_i = 0$  otherwise. Then, the verification algorithm computes and outputs  $C(\beta_1, \ldots, \beta_m)$ .

Weak predictability of 1-element laconic arguments. We now show that argument systems with a generic verification procedure and where the proof consists of a single group element must be predictable. We do this in two steps. First, we show that any 1-element laconic argument satisfies a notion of "weak predictability," where we essentially allow there to be up to k accepting proofs (Definition 5.6). Second, we show that any laconic argument that is weakly predictable is also predictable, but with a larger completeness error (Theorem 5.9).

<span id="page-32-0"></span>**Definition 5.6** (Weakly-Predictable Laconic Argument). Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}})$  be a 2-message laconic argument for a relation  $\mathcal{R}$ , and let T be the codomain of  $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  (i.e., the set of possible proof strings). We say that  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}}$  is k-weakly predictable if there exists an efficient predictor algorithm  $\mathsf{LA}_{\mathsf{predict}}$  that on input any verification state  $\mathsf{st}$  in the support of  $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}$ , outputs a (possibly empty) set of proofs  $S \subseteq T$  with the following two properties:

- Either  $|S| \le k$  or  $S \ge |T| k$ .
- $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st},\pi) = 1$  if and only if  $\pi \in S$ .

<span id="page-32-2"></span>**Theorem 5.7** (Weak Predictability of 1-Element Laconic Arguments). Every 1-element laconic argument for a relation  $\mathcal{R}$  (that is computable by a Boolean circuit of size  $\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ ) with a generic verification algorithm (Definition 5.5) is k-weakly predictable for  $k = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ .

*Proof.* Let  $\Pi_{LA} = (\mathcal{Q}_{LA}, \mathcal{P}_{LA}, \mathcal{V}_{LA})$  be a 1-element laconic argument with a generic verification procedure. We construct a predictor algorithm  $\mathsf{LA}_{\mathsf{predict}}$  for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}}$  that works as follows:

1. On input  $\mathsf{st} = ((\mathbb{G}, p, g), \{a_i, b_i\}_{i \in [m]}, C)$  where  $0 \neq a_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$  and  $b_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$ , compute  $\pi_i \leftarrow a_i^{-1}b_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$  for each  $i \in [m]$ . Note that the  $a_1, \ldots, a_m$  are scalars in this case because the proof consists of a single group element.

{33}------------------------------------------------

- 2. Initialize a set  $T \leftarrow \emptyset$ . For each  $i \in [m]$ , add  $g^{\pi_i} \in \mathbb{G}$  to T if  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}, g^{\pi_i}) = 1$ .
- 3. Finally, take any  $\pi' \in \mathbb{F}_p \setminus \{\pi_1, \dots, \pi_m\}$ . If no such  $\pi'$  exists or  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}, g^{\pi'}) = 0$ , output S = T. Otherwise, output the set  $S = T \cup (\mathbb{G} \setminus \{g^{\pi_1}, \dots, g^{\pi_m}\})$ .

It suffices to argue that  $\mathsf{LA}_{\mathsf{predict}}$  satisfies each of the required properties. Since  $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  is efficient, we have that  $m = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , and so  $\mathsf{LA}_{\mathsf{predict}}$  is efficient. By construction, the output S of  $\mathsf{LA}_{\mathsf{predict}}$  satisfies  $|S| \leq m$  or  $|S| \geq |\mathbb{G}| - m$ , so the first property is satisfied. For the second property, take any candidate proof  $g^{\pi} \in \mathbb{G}$  (where  $\pi \in \mathbb{F}_p$ ). We consider two cases:

- Suppose  $\pi \in {\pi_1, \ldots, \pi_m}$ . Then,  $g^{\pi} \in T$  if and only if  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}, g^{\pi}) = 1$ . Since  $T \subseteq S$ , the claim holds.
- Suppose  $\pi \notin \{\pi_1, \dots, \pi_m\}$ . This means that  $a_i \pi \neq b_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$  for all  $i \in [m]$ . By definition then,  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}, g^\pi) = C(0^m) = \mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}, g^{\pi'})$ . Correspondingly,  $g^\pi \in S$  if and only if  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}, g^{\pi'}) = 1 = \mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}, g^\pi)$ , and again, the claim holds.

From weak predictability to predictability. Now we show that any laconic argument that is weakly predictable is predictable (Definition 5.3), though with a much larger completeness error. The construction is simple: to obtain a standard predictable argument from a weakly-predictable one, the query-generation algorithm samples one of the k possible proofs in the weakly-predictable scheme at random as its only accepting proof. While this transformation substantially increases the completeness error, it still suffices to obtain witness encryption (as long as  $k = \text{poly}(\lambda)$ ). Combining these results with Theorem 5.4, we show that any 1-element laconic argument for an NP language  $\mathcal{L}$  where verification is generic and which has negligible soundness error implies a witness encryption for  $\mathcal{L}$  (Corollary 5.10).

<span id="page-33-1"></span>Construction 5.8 (Predictable Argument from Weakly-Predictable Argument). Let  $\Pi'_{LA} = (\mathcal{Q}'_{LA}, \mathcal{P}'_{LA}, \mathcal{V}'_{LA})$  be a k-weakly predictable 2-message laconic argument for a relation  $\mathcal{R}$ , and let  $LA'_{predict}$  be the predictor algorithm for  $\Pi'_{LA}$ . We construct a predictable laconic argument  $\Pi_{LA} = (\mathcal{Q}_{LA}, \mathcal{P}_{LA}, \mathcal{V}_{LA})$  for  $\mathcal{R}$  as follows:

- $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda}, x)$ : On input the security parameter  $\lambda$  and a statement x, the query-generation algorithm computes  $(\mathsf{st}', q') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda}, x)$  and runs  $S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}')$ . If  $1 \leq |S| \leq k$ , it samples  $\pi \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} S$  and outputs the query q = q' and the state  $\mathsf{st} = \pi$ . If |S| = 0, it samples  $\pi \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} T$  and outputs the query q = q' and the state  $\mathsf{st} = \pi$ . Finally, if |S| > k, it output the query  $q = \bot$  and the state  $\mathsf{st} = \bot$ .
- $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}(q, x, w)$ : On input a query q, the statement x, and a witness w, the prover algorithm outputs the proof  $\pi = \bot$  if  $q = \bot$ . Otherwise, it computes and outputs  $\pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(q, x, w)$ .
- $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st},\pi)$ : On input the verification state  $\mathsf{st}$  and a proof  $\pi$ , the verification algorithm outputs 1 if  $\pi = \mathsf{st}$  and 0 otherwise.

<span id="page-33-0"></span>**Theorem 5.9** (Predictable Argument from Weakly-Predictable Argument). Let  $\Pi'_{\mathsf{LA}}$  be a k-weakly predictable argument for a relation  $\mathcal{R}$  (with associated language  $\mathcal{L}$ ) with completeness error c' and soundness error  $\varepsilon'$ . Let T be the codomain of  $\mathcal{P}'_{\mathsf{LA}}$  and suppose that  $|T| \geq 2 \cdot k$ . Then the laconic argument  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}}$  from Construction 5.8 is a predictable argument for  $\mathcal{R}$  with completeness error c = 1 - (1 - c')/k and soundness error  $\varepsilon = 3 \cdot \varepsilon' + 1/|T|$ .

*Proof.* By construction,  $\Pi_{LA}$  is predictable, so it suffices to argue completeness and soundness:

• Completeness: Take any  $(x, w) \in \mathcal{R}$ . By definition, the query-generation algorithm  $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda}, x)$  starts by computing  $S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}')$  where  $(\mathsf{st}', q') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda}, x)$ . Since  $\Pi'_{\mathsf{LA}}$  is k-weakly predictable, this means that either  $|S| \leq k$  or |S| > k. We define the following probabilities:

$$\begin{split} \delta_{1} &\coloneqq \Pr[|S| \leq k \mid (\mathsf{st}',q') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda},x); S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}')] \\ \delta_{2} &\coloneqq \Pr[|S| > k \mid (\mathsf{st}',q') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda},x); S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}')] \\ p_{1} &\coloneqq \Pr[\mathcal{V}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}',x,\pi) = 1 \land |S| \leq k \mid (\mathsf{st}',q') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda},x); S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}'); \pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(x,q')] \\ p_{2} &\coloneqq \Pr[\mathcal{V}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}',x,\pi) = 1 \land |S| > k \mid (\mathsf{st}',q') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda},x); S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}'); \pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(x,q')] \end{split}$$

{34}------------------------------------------------

Note that if |S| = 0, then  $\mathcal{V}'_{\mathsf{LA}}$  never accepts, so by definition,  $p_1 + p_2 \ge 1 - c'$ . It suffices to consider the following two cases:

- If  $1 \leq |S| \leq k$ , then q = q' and the prover algorithm computes the proof as  $\pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(q, x, w)$ . This means that  $\mathcal{V}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}', x, \pi) = 1$  with probability  $p_1/\delta_1$ . By definition of S, this means that  $\pi \in S$  with probability at least  $p_1/\delta_1$ . Since  $\mathsf{st}$  is sampled uniformly at random from S and  $|S| \leq k$ , we have that

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st},\pi)=1]=\Pr[\pi=\mathsf{st}]\geq p_1/(\delta k).$$

- If |S| > k, then  $q = \bot$  and the prover algorithm computes the proof as  $\pi = \bot$ . In this case, the verifier accepts with probability 1.

The verifier thus accepts with probability at least  $p_1/(\delta_1 k) \cdot \delta_1 + \delta_2 \ge p_1/k + p_2 \ge (p_1 + p_2)/k \ge (1 - c')/k$ . The completeness error is then 1 - (1 - c')/k.

- Soundness: Let  $\mathcal{P}^*$  be any polynomial-size prover for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}}$ . Take any  $x \notin \mathcal{L}$ , and consider  $(q, \mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda}, x)$ . By definition,  $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  first computes  $(q', \mathsf{st}') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda}, x)$  and  $S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}')$ . We consider two possibilities:
  - If |S| = 0 then st is a random element from T and independent of the query q = q'. Thus, the probability that the proof  $\pi$  output by the prover satisfies  $\pi = \text{st}$  is at most 1/|T|.
  - If  $1 \leq |S| \leq k$ , then q = q'. By soundness of  $\Pi'_{1,A}$

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}',\pi) = 1 \mid (q',\mathsf{st}') \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda},x), \pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}^*(1^{\lambda},x,q')] \leq \varepsilon'.$$

By construction of  $\mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}$ , we have that  $\pi \in S$  if and only if  $\mathcal{V}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}',\pi) = 1$ . Thus, with probability at most  $\varepsilon'$ , the proof  $\pi$  output by  $\mathcal{P}^*$  in this case is contained in S. Correspondingly, the probability that  $\pi = \mathsf{st} \in S$  is at most  $\varepsilon'$ .

- Suppose |S| > k. By definition of weak predictability, this means that  $|S| \ge |T| - k$ . We argue that this case occurs with probability at most  $2\varepsilon'$  (over the randomness of query-generation). Suppose otherwise, and consider a prover for  $\Pi'_{\mathsf{LA}}$  that simply outputs a random proof string  $\pi \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} T$ . If  $|S| \ge |T| - k$ , then with probability  $|S|/|T| \ge (|T| - k)/|T| \ge 1/2$ , we have that  $\pi \in S$ , and  $\mathcal{V}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st}',\pi)$  outputs 1. Thus, if  $\mathcal{Q}'_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^\lambda,x)$  outputs  $\mathsf{st}'$  such that  $S \leftarrow \mathsf{LA}'_{\mathsf{predict}}(\mathsf{st}')$  satisfies  $|S| \ge k$  with probability at least  $2\varepsilon'$ , then this prover breaks soundness of  $\Pi'_{\mathsf{LA}}$  with probability at least  $\varepsilon'$ , which is a contradiction. Hence, this case can only happen with probability at most  $2\varepsilon'$ .

We conclude that if  $\Pi'_{\mathsf{LA}}$  has soundness error  $\varepsilon'$ , then  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}}$  has soundness error at most  $3\varepsilon' + 1/|T|$ .  $\square$ 

<span id="page-34-0"></span>Corollary 5.10 (Witness Encryption from 1-Element Laconic Argument). Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}})$  be a laconic argument for an NP language  $\mathcal{L}$  where the proof consists of a single group element and the verification algorithm is generic (Definition 5.5). If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}}$  has completeness error  $c = c(\lambda)$  where  $1 - c \geq 1/\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  and negligible soundness error  $\varepsilon(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , then there exists a witness encryption scheme for  $\mathcal{L}$  with statistical correctness.

Proof. This follows by combining Theorems 5.4, 5.7 and 5.9. Namely, by Theorem 5.7,  $\Pi_{LA}$  is k-weakly predictable for some  $k = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ . From Construction 5.8 and Theorem 5.9, we obtain a predictable laconic argument  $\Pi'_{LA}$  for  $\mathcal{L}$  with completeness error c' = 1 - (1 - c)/k and soundness  $\varepsilon' = 3\varepsilon + 1/|\mathbb{G}|$ , where  $\mathbb{G}$  is the group associated with  $\Pi_{LA}$ . Since  $\varepsilon$  is negligible, so is  $\varepsilon'$  (since  $1/|\mathbb{G}|$  is negligible). Thus, we can apply Theorem 5.4 to  $\Pi'_{LA}$  to obtain a witness encryption scheme for  $\mathcal{L}$  with correctness error c'/2 = 1/2 - (1-c)/(2k). Since  $(1-c) \geq 1/\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  and  $k = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , this means that  $c'/2 = 1/2 - 1/\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , so we can use parallel repetition to amplify correctness (Remark 5.2).

{35}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-35-1"></span>5.2 1-Element Laconic Argument from Hardness of Approximation

In this section, we show that under a hardness of approximation hypothesis for the minimum distance problem, which strengthens known hardness results [DMS99], we can construct a 1-element laconic argument for general NP languages. We begin by recalling the minimum distance problem and then stating our hardness of approximation hypothesis.

**Definition 5.11** (Gap Minimum Distance Problem (GapMDP) [DMS99, Definition 1]). For an approximation factor  $\beta$ , an instance of the GapMDP<sub> $\beta$ </sub> problem is a pair ( $\mathbf{A}, d$ ) where  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{F}^{n \times k}$  for some finite field  $\mathbb{F}$  and  $d \in \mathbb{N}$  such that

- $(\mathbf{A}, d)$  is a YES instance if  $\operatorname{dist}(\mathbf{A}) \leq d$ .
- $(\mathbf{A}, d)$  is a NO instance if  $\operatorname{dist}(\mathbf{A}) \geq \beta \cdot d$ .

Here,  $\operatorname{\mathsf{dist}}(\mathbf{A})$  is the minimum distance (under the Hamming metric) of the code generated by  $\mathbf{A}$ . We define the  $\operatorname{\mathsf{GapMDP}}_{\beta}$  relation to be the NP relation associated with this promise problem (see Section 2 for a formal definition) that takes as input an instance  $(\mathbf{A}, d)$  where  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{F}^{n \times k}$ , and a witness  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^k$ , and outputs 1 if the following two properties hold:

- $0 < \mathsf{wt}(\mathbf{v}) \le d$ ;
- $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^k$  is a codeword in the code generated by  $\mathbf{A}$ .

<span id="page-35-0"></span>**Hypothesis 5.12** (Hardness of Approximation for GapMDP). For some  $\beta = \omega(\log n)$ , the GapMDP<sub> $\beta$ </sub> relation is NP-hard for any choice of finite field  $\mathbb F$  where  $|\mathbb F| = 2^{O(n)}$ . Specifically, there exists a deterministic Karp-Levin reduction from SAT to the NP relation associated with the GapMDP<sub> $\beta$ </sub> promise problem,<sup>14</sup> where the reduction algorithm takes a target field  $\mathbb F$  as an *explicit* input and outputs an instance  $(\mathbf A, d)$  over  $\mathbb F$  in time  $\mathsf{poly}(n, \log |\mathbb F|)$ .

**Existing hardness results on GapMDP.** Dumer et al. [DMS99] showed that the GapMDP was NP-hard for any constant approximation factor  $\beta = O(1)$  (over any polynomial-size field) via a randomized reduction. Subsequently, Cheng and Wan |CW09| as well as Austrin and Khot |AK14| gave a deterministic reduction for the same parameter regimes. The latter results additionally give a deterministic quasi-polynomial time reduction from NP to the  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  for any  $\beta = 2^{\log^{1-\varepsilon}(n)}$  (i.e., unless  $\mathsf{NP} \subseteq \mathsf{DTIME}(2^{\mathsf{polylog}(n)})$ , there is no polynomial-time algorithm for  $GapMDP_{\beta}$ ). In our setting, we need to strengthen the existing hardness of approximation results in two different directions: (1) Hypothesis 5.12 requires a deterministic polynomial time reduction to GapMDP while the existing reductions in the super-constant regime are all quasi-polynomial; and (2) we require that the reduction applies to large prime characteristic fields (i.e., fields that are superpolynomial in the instance size). While existing reductions are agnostic about the choice of the field, the running time of existing reductions scale polynomially in the characteristic of the field, so they do not directly generalize to super-polynomial size fields. While existing techniques do not suffice for proving Hypothesis 5.12, there are no known barriers to doing so [Kho20]. Finally, we note that existing reductions from  $\mathsf{NP}$  to  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_\beta$  are Karp-Levin reductions; namely, existing reductions show how to map  $\mathsf{NP}$  instances to  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_\beta$  instances (that respect the promise), and moreover, construct an explicit witness for  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_\beta$ from a witness for the NP-hard problem.

<span id="page-35-4"></span>Construction 5.13 (Laconic Argument for GapMDP). Let  $\lambda$  be a security parameter,  $\varepsilon > 0$  be a completeness parameter, and  $\beta > 0$  be the approximation factor. Let GroupGen be a prime-order group generator, and let  $p = p(\lambda)$  be the order of the group output by GroupGen. We construct a two-message laconic argument  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LA}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}, \mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}})$  for  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  (for instances over  $\mathbb{F}_p$ ):

<span id="page-35-2"></span> $<sup>\</sup>overline{\ ^{14}}$ We refer to Section 2 for a formal definition of Karp-Levin reductions from an NP problem to an NP promise problem.

<span id="page-35-3"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>15</sup>We formulate the hypothesis for  $|\mathbb{F}| = 2^{O(n)}$ , although any field of super-polynomial size would also suffice.

{36}------------------------------------------------

- $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda}, (\mathbf{A}, d))$ : On input the security parameter  $\lambda$  and an  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  instance  $(\mathbf{A}, d)$  over  $\mathbb{F}_p$ , the query algorithm samples  $(\mathbb{G}, p, g) \leftarrow \mathsf{GroupGen}(1^{\lambda})$ . Let  $\mathbf{H} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{\ell \times k}$  be the parity check matrix for the code generated by  $\mathbf{A}$ . Then the verifier constructs the following components:
  - Sample a random vector  $\mathbf{e} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$  where each component  $e_i = 0$  with probability  $1 \varepsilon/d$  and  $e_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$  otherwise.
  - Sample  $\mathbf{c} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^k$ ,  $\mathbf{r} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^\ell$ ,  $s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$  and compute  $\mathbf{z}^\mathsf{T} = \mathbf{r}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{H} + s \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} + \mathbf{e}^\mathsf{T} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$ .

The algorithm outputs  $((\mathbb{G}, p, g), \mathbf{c}, g^{\mathbf{z}})$  as its query and  $\mathsf{st} = g^s$  as its state. Here, we write  $g^{\mathbf{z}}$  to denote the vector of group elements  $(g^{z_1}, \ldots, g^{z_k})$ , where  $z_1, \ldots, z_k$  are the components of  $\mathbf{z}$ .

- $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}(q, x, w)$ : On input a query  $q = ((\mathbb{G}, p, g), \mathbf{c}, g^{\mathbf{z}})$ , a GapMDP instance  $(\mathbf{A}, d)$  and a witness  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$ , the prover algorithm does the following:
  - If  $\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{v} = 0$ , then the prover aborts with output  $\bot$ . Otherwise, let  $t = (\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{v})^{-1}$ .
  - Output the proof  $\pi = g^{t \cdot \mathbf{z}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v}}$  (which can be computed from  $t, \mathbf{v}$ , and  $g^{\mathbf{z}}$ ).
- $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st},\pi)$ : On input the verification state  $\mathsf{st} \in \mathbb{G}$  and a proof  $\pi \in \mathbb{G}$ , output 1 if  $\mathsf{st} = \pi$ , and 0 otherwise.

Completeness and soundness analysis. We now state the completeness and soundness theorems for Construction 5.13 as well as the resulting implication to 1-element laconic arguments and witness encryption for NP in the generic group model. We defer the completeness and soundness proofs to Section 5.3.

<span id="page-36-2"></span>**Theorem 5.14** (Completeness). Construction 5.13 has completeness error  $\varepsilon$ .

<span id="page-36-3"></span>**Theorem 5.15** (Soundness). If  $\varepsilon\beta = \omega(\log n)$  and GroupGen is modeled as a generic group, then Construction 5.13 has soundness error  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-36-1"></span>Corollary 5.16 (1-Element Laconic Argument for NP). Let  $\lambda$  be a security parameter. Under Hypothesis 5.12, there exists a predictable laconic argument for NP in the generic group model with completeness error o(1) and soundness error  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$  and where the prover's message consists of a single group element.

Proof. Let  $\beta(\lambda) = f(\lambda) \log \lambda$  where  $f(\lambda) = \omega(1)$  for which Hypothesis 5.12 holds. Take  $\varepsilon = 1/\sqrt{f(\lambda)} = o(1)$ . By instantiating Construction 5.13 with this choice of  $\beta, \varepsilon$  and appealing to Theorems 5.14 and 5.15, we obtain a laconic argument for  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  with completeness error  $\varepsilon = o(1)$  and soundness error  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . In addition, Construction 5.13 is predictable by construction. Finally, by Hypothesis 5.12, there exists a deterministic polynomial-time Karp-Levin reduction from NP to  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$ , and so we can use our laconic argument for  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  to obtain a laconic argument for any NP language.

Corollary 5.17 (Hypothetical Witness Encryption for NP in the Generic Group Model). Under Hypothesis 5.12, there exists a witness encryption scheme for NP in the generic group model.

*Proof.* Follows by instantiating Theorem 5.4 with the predictable laconic argument from Corollary 5.16 (and applying the correctness amplification approach from Remark 5.2).  $\Box$ 

<span id="page-36-0"></span>**Remark 5.18** (Linear PCP for GapMDP). We note that Construction 5.13 implicitly constructs a 2-query (instance-dependent) linear PCP  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}})$  for the  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  problem:

- $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathbf{A},d)$ : On input a  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  instance  $(\mathbf{A},d)$  over a finite field  $\mathbb{F}$ , let  $\mathbf{H} \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell \times k}$  be the parity check matrix for the code generated by  $\mathbf{A}$ . Sample  $\mathbf{c} \leftarrow^{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{F}^k$   $\mathbf{r} \leftarrow^{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{F}^\ell$ ,  $\mathbf{e} \in \mathbb{F}^k$  and  $s \leftarrow^{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{F}$  as in Construction 5.13. Output the queries  $\mathbf{q}_1^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{r}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{H} + s\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}} + \mathbf{e}^{\mathsf{T}} \in \mathbb{F}^k$  and  $\mathbf{q}_2^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}} \in \mathbb{F}^k$  and the state  $\mathsf{st} = s$ .
- $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}((\mathbf{A},d),\mathbf{v})$ : On input an instance  $(\mathbf{A},d)$  and a vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^k$ , output the proof  $\boldsymbol{\pi} = \mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^k$ .

{37}------------------------------------------------

•  $\mathcal{V}_{LPCP}(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{a})$ : On input a state  $\mathsf{st} = s$  and responses  $\mathbf{a} = (a_1, a_2) \in \mathbb{F}^2$ , the verifier accepts if  $a_1 \neq 0$  and  $a_1 = s \cdot a_2$ , and rejects otherwise.

Completeness and soundness of this linear PCP follows via a similar analysis as in the proof of Theorems 5.14 and 5.15.

<span id="page-37-0"></span>Remark 5.19 (Witness Encryption via GapMWSP). A natural question is whether we might be able to obtain a predictable argument (and correspondingly, a witness encryption scheme) for NP starting from the 1-query linear PCP for GapMWSP from Construction 4.3. While the 1-query linear PCP in Construction 4.3 is a predictable 1-query linear PCP, we do not have a generic compiler that takes a 1-query linear PCP and compiles it into a 1-element argument system (the [BCI+13] compiler with ElGamal encryption yields a 2-element non-predictable argument). Moreover, the general approach from Construction 5.13 of encoding the linear PCP query in the "exponent" does not yield a sound argument system when applied to Construction 4.3. Specifically, the soundness analysis in Construction 5.13 critically relied on the noise vector  $\mathbf{e}$  being hidden from the view of the prover. If we directly embed the linear PCP query  $\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{A} + \mathbf{e}^{\mathsf{T}}$  from Construction 4.3 in the exponent (i.e., we take the verifier message to be  $g^{\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{A}+\mathbf{e}^{\mathsf{T}}}$ ), then a malicious prover who can find a vector  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{F}^n$  with low Hamming weight where  $\mathbf{A}\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{0}$  can compute  $g^{\mathbf{e}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{x}}$ , which leaks information about the indices of the non-zero components of  $\mathbf{e}$ . This is not an issue for Construction 5.13 since for false instances, the GapMDP problem stipulates that no such low density vectors  $\mathbf{x}$  exist.

<span id="page-37-1"></span>Remark 5.20 (Hyperplane Obfuscation and Witness Encryption). An alternative approach to obtaining a witness encryption scheme for the GapMWSP problem is to obfuscate the predictable 1-query linear PCP from Construction 4.3. In more detail, suppose we want to encrypt a message m for the GapMWSP instance  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{b}, d)$ . To do so, the encrypter first samples a linear PCP query  $\mathbf{q} \in \mathbb{F}_p$  for the GapMWSP instance together with the expected response  $t \in \mathbb{F}$  by running the query-generation algorithm from Construction 4.3. Then, the encrypter constructs the program  $P_{\mathbf{q},t,m}(\pi)$  that on input  $\pi \in \mathbb{F}^n$  outputs m if  $\mathbf{q}^T\pi = t$  and outputs  $\perp$  otherwise. The ciphertext is an obfuscation of the program  $P_{\mathbf{q},t,m}$ . To decrypt, the decrypter constructs a linear PCP proof  $\pi \in \mathbb{F}^n$  for the instance  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{b}, d)$  and runs the program on input  $\pi$ . Correctness of the encryption scheme then follows by completeness of the linear PCP, while semantic security follows from soundness of the linear PCP and assuming that the obfuscated program completely hides  $(\mathbf{q}, t, m)$ , up to what can be deduced from the input-output behavior of the program  $P_{\mathbf{q},t,m}$ . For instance if we have an ideal or virtual black-box obfuscation (VBB obfuscation) [BGI^+01] of  $P_{\mathbf{q},t,m}$ , then we can leverage Construction 4.3 to obtain a witness encryption for GapMWSP, and by Lemma 4.2, for all of NP.

While VBB obfuscation for general circuits is impossible in the standard model |BGI<sup>+</sup>01|, we do have positive results for specific families function families such as point functions [Can 97, Wee 05a, CD 08], linear subspaces [CRV10], conjunctions [BVWW16], and more recently, "compute-and-compare" programs [WZ17, GKW17] (also called "lockable obfuscation"). In our setting, the program  $P_{\mathbf{q},t,m}$  is simple and corresponds to membership testing in a secret affine hyperplane in  $\mathbb{F}^n$  (i.e., the set of solutions  $\pi \in \mathbb{F}^n$  to the affine relation  $\mathbf{q}^{\mathsf{T}}\boldsymbol{\pi}=t$ ). More broadly, the program  $P_{\mathbf{q},t,m}$  is an example of a compute-and-compare program (i.e., a program that first performs a computation (e.g.,  $\mathbf{q}^{\mathsf{T}}\boldsymbol{\pi}$ ) and outputs a message only if the result of the program matches a target value t). However, existing constructions of lockable obfuscation and computeand-compare obfuscation require that the target value be computationally unpredictable even given the underlying function. In our case, this means that the target t should be computationally unpredictable even given the query q, which does not hold in our setting. With respect to the obfuscation scheme for hyperplane membership from [CRV10], the construction only support linear subspaces (i.e., the case where the target value is 0) and does not extend to affine hyperplane membership testing. Nonetheless, we believe this to be an interesting connection between witness encryption for NP and obfuscation for affine hyperplane membership testing and may provide new avenues for constructing witness encryption for NP (without relying on Hypothesis 5.12).

## <span id="page-37-2"></span>5.3 Analysis of Construction 5.13.

In this section, we give the completeness and soundness analysis of Construction 5.13.

{38}------------------------------------------------

**Proof of Theorem 5.14 (Completeness).** Let  $(\mathbf{A}, d)$  be a  $\mathsf{GapMDP}_{\beta}$  instance with solution  $\mathbf{0} \neq \mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$ ; namely,  $\mathbf{H}\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}$  and  $\mathbf{wt}(\mathbf{v}) \leq d$ . Let  $((\mathbb{G}, p, g), \mathbf{c}, g^{\mathbf{z}})$  be the verifier's query. Since  $\mathbf{v} \neq \mathbf{0}$  and  $\mathbf{c}$  is uniform over  $\mathbb{F}_p^k$ , we can apply Schwartz-Zippel lemma to conclude that  $\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v} \neq 0$  except with probability  $1/|\mathbb{F}_p| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Thus, the prover can successfully compute  $t = (\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v})^{-1}$  and the proof  $\pi = g^{t \cdot \mathbf{z}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v}}$  from t,  $\mathbf{v}$ , and  $g^{\mathbf{z}}$ . By construction,

$$\mathbf{z}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{r}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{H} \mathbf{v} + s \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v} + \mathbf{e}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v} = s \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v} + \mathbf{e}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v},$$

since  $\mathbf{H}\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}$ . Next, we claim that with probability at least  $1 - \varepsilon$ ,  $\mathbf{e}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v} = 0$ . Since  $\mathsf{wt}(\mathbf{v}) \leq d$  and each component of  $\mathbf{e}$  is zero with probability  $1 - \varepsilon/d$ , we have that

$$\Pr[\mathbf{e}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{v} = 0] \ge (1 - \varepsilon/d)^{\mathsf{wt}(\mathbf{v})} \ge (1 - \varepsilon/d)^d \ge 1 - \varepsilon$$

for  $d \geq 1$ . Thus, with probability at least  $1 - \varepsilon$ ,

$$\pi = g^{t \cdot \mathbf{z}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v}} = g^{(\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v})^{-1} s(\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v})} = g^s = \mathsf{st},$$

and the verifier accepts.

**Proof of Theorem 5.15 (Soundness).** Take any NO instance  $(\mathbf{A}, d)$ , and let  $\mathbf{H}$  be the parity-check matrix for the code generated by  $\mathbf{A}$ . We use a hybrid argument:

- Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the real soundness experiment where we replace the group  $(\mathbb{G}, p, g)$  with oracle access to a generic group  $\mathcal{G}$ . The challenger runs  $(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}, p) \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ . It constructs  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$  as in  $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  and computes  $h_i \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, z_i)$ , where  $z_i$  denotes the  $i^{\mathsf{th}}$  components of  $\mathbf{z}$ . It also computes  $\mathsf{st} \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, s)$  and  $g \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, 1)$ . The challenger gives  $((\mathsf{pp}, p, g), \mathbf{c}, h_1, \ldots, h_k)$  to the prover. The prover is then given access to the generic group oracles  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}$ ,  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}$ ,  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}$ ,  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Test}$ . At the end of the game, the prover outputs a proof  $\pi \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , and the challenger outputs 1 if  $\pi$  and  $\mathsf{st}$  encode the same value (by using  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}$  and  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Test}$ ), and 0 otherwise.
- Hyb<sub>1</sub>: In this experiment, we change how the generic group oracle queries are implemented. Specifically, the challenger begins by sampling  $(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}, p) \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda)$  as in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ . In this experiment, the challenger will explicitly maintain the mapping T of encodings to handles. The challenger samples encodings  $g \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^\lambda$ ,  $h_1, \ldots, h_k \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^\lambda$  and st  $\overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^\lambda$  and adds mappings  $g \mapsto 1$ ,  $h_i \mapsto \hat{z}_i$ , st  $\mapsto \hat{s}$ , where  $\hat{z}_1, \ldots, \hat{z}_k$ , and  $\hat{s}$  are formal variables (see Remark C.3). It also samples  $\mathbf{c} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^k$  as in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ . The challenger gives  $((\mathsf{pp}, p, g), \mathbf{c}, h_1, \ldots, h_k)$  to the prover. The prover is then given access to the generic group oracles, which are implemented as follows:
  - GGM.Setup: The challenger always replies with  $\perp$ .
  - GGM.Encode: The challenger always replies with  $\perp$ .
  - GGM.Add: On input a key k and handles  $\xi_1, \xi_2 \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the challenger checks that k = pp and handles  $\xi_1, \xi_2$  are present in T and mapped to formal polynomials  $f_1, f_2$  over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle samples a fresh handle  $\xi \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , adds the entry  $\xi \mapsto (f_1 + f_2)$  to T, and replies with  $\xi$ .
  - GGM.Test: On input a key k and a handle  $\xi$ , the oracle checks that k = pp, that  $\xi$  is present in T and mapped to a formal polynomial f over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle outputs "zero" if  $f \equiv 0$  is the identically-zero polynomial over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  and "nonzero" otherwise.

At the end of the game, after the prover outputs its proof  $\pi$ , the output of the experiment is 1 if  $\pi$  and st encode identical polynomials over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  (i.e.,  $\pi \mapsto f_1$  and st  $\mapsto f_2$  such that  $f_1 - f_2 \equiv 0$  over  $\mathbb{F}_p$ ). Otherwise, the output of the experiment is 0.

•  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ : Same as  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  except the challenger samples  $\mathsf{st} \overset{\mathtt{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  and adds the mapping  $\mathsf{st} \mapsto s$  after the adversary has outputted its proof. The output is still computed as in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ .

{39}------------------------------------------------

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output of experiment  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ .

<span id="page-39-0"></span>**Lemma 5.21.** For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  that make a polynomial number of queries to  $\mathcal{G}$ ,  $|\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A}) = 1] - \Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A}) = 1]| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

*Proof.* First, the components  $(pp, p, g, \mathbf{c}, h_1, \dots, h_k)$  are identically distributed in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ . Thus, it suffices to show that each of the adversary's queries to the generic group oracle are statistically indistinguishable. We use a hybrid argument over the number of queries the adversary makes, where in the  $i^{\text{th}}$  hybrid  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$ , the first i queries are answered according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  while the remaining queries are answered according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ . We show that for all i, the outputs of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$  are statistically indistinguishable. It suffices then to consider the  $i^{\text{th}}$  query:

- GGM.Setup: In both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$ , the setup oracle outputs  $\bot$ .
- GGM.Encode: In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ , the GGM.Encode oracle outputs  $\bot$  unless the adversary queries the oracle on  $k = \mathsf{sk}$ . Since the view of  $\mathcal{A}$  in the first i-1 queries in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  is independent of  $\mathsf{sk}$  and  $\mathsf{sk}$  is uniform over  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the output is  $\bot$  with probability  $1-2^{-\lambda}$  (namely, the secret key  $\mathsf{sk}$  can be sampled after the adversary's query). In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$ , the output of GGM.Encode on the  $i^{\mathsf{th}}$  query is always  $\bot$ , so the output of GGM.Encode is statistically indistinguishable in the two experiments.
- GGM.Add: The addition oracle has identical behavior in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$ .
- GGM.Test: Suppose an adversary makes a query to the GGM.Test oracle on k = pp and a handle  $\xi \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . First, if  $k \neq pp$  or  $\xi \notin T$ , then the output in both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$  is  $\bot$ , so it suffices to consider the case where k = pp and  $\xi \in T$ . This means that  $\xi$  must have been added to T as a result of a GGM.Encode query or as a result of a GGM.Add query. By construction, the only values in T from the encoding algorithm correspond to the encodings of  $1, \hat{z}_1, \ldots, \hat{z}_k$ , and  $\hat{s}$ . Any other encodings in T must be the outcome of invoking GGM.Add on the existing elements. By an inductive argument over the GGM.Add queries, we can argue that every valid handle  $\xi$  in T necessarily corresponds to a linear function in the values  $1, \hat{z}_1, \ldots, \hat{z}_k$ , and  $\hat{s}$ . Thus, any valid zero-test query the adversary makes can be expressed as

$$f(\hat{z}_1,\ldots,\hat{z}_k,\hat{s}) \coloneqq \sum_{i\in[k]} \alpha_i \hat{z}_i + \gamma \hat{s} + \delta,$$

for some choice of scalars  $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_k, \gamma, \delta \in \mathbb{F}_p$ . If  $f \equiv 0$  is the identically-zero polynomial, then the output in both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$  is "zero." It suffices to consider the case where  $f \not\equiv 0$ . In this case, the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$  is always "nonzero," while the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  depends on the value of f at the concrete values of  $z_1, \ldots, z_k, s$  sampled at the beginning of the experiment. Note that by construction, the adversary's view in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  in the first i-1 queries is independent of the values of  $\mathbf{r}$ ,  $\mathbf{e}$ , and s. Thus, we can sample the values of  $\mathbf{r}$ ,  $\mathbf{e}$ , s after the adversary has chosen its  $i^{\mathrm{th}}$  query.

First, let  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$  be the vector whose components are  $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_k$ . Then, we can write

$$f(z_1, \ldots, z_k, s) = \mathbf{z}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\alpha} + \gamma s + \delta = \mathbf{r}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{H} \boldsymbol{\alpha} + \mathbf{e}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\alpha} + (\gamma + \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\alpha}) s + \delta.$$

We can re-write  $f(z_1, \ldots, z_k, s)$  as a linear polynomial g in the variables  $\mathbf{r}$ , s, and  $\mathbf{e}$ :

$$g(\mathbf{r}, s, \mathbf{e}) = \mathbf{r}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{H} \boldsymbol{\alpha} + \mathbf{e}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\alpha} + (\gamma + \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\alpha}) s + \delta = f(z_1, \dots, z_k, s)$$

We now consider two cases:

- Suppose that  $\alpha = \mathbf{0}$ . In this case, the polynomial  $g(\mathbf{r}, s, \mathbf{e}) = \gamma s + \delta$ . If g is not identically zero, then at least one of  $\gamma, \delta \neq 0$ . If  $\gamma \neq 0$ , this is a nonzero polynomial in s. Since s is uniform over  $\mathbb{F}_p$ , we apply Schwartz-Zippel to conclude that the probability that  $g(\mathbf{r}, s, \mathbf{e}) = 0$  is  $1/|\mathbb{F}_p| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . With overwhelming probability, the zero-test oracle outputs "nonzero." If  $\gamma = 0$  and  $\delta \neq 0$ , then g is a constant nonzero polynomial, and the zero-test oracle always outputs "nonzero."

{40}------------------------------------------------

- Suppose that  $\alpha \neq 0$  but  $\mathbf{H}\alpha = 0$ . Since  $(\mathbf{A}, d)$  is a NO instance, it must be the case that  $\mathsf{wt}(\alpha) \geq \beta \cdot d$ . We argue in this case that  $\mathbf{e}^\mathsf{T}\alpha$  is not identically-zero with overwhelming probability. By construction,  $\Pr[e_i = 0] = 1 - \varepsilon/d$  for each  $i \in [k]$ , and each component  $e_i$  is independent and identically distributed. Let  $S_\alpha = \{i \in [k] : \alpha_i \neq 0\}$  be the set of indices in  $\alpha$  that are non-zero. As argued above,  $|S_\alpha| = \mathsf{wt}(\alpha) \geq \beta \cdot d$ . Thus, the probability that  $e_i = 0$  for all indices  $i \in S_\alpha$  is bounded by

$$\Pr[\forall i \in S_{\alpha} : e_i = 0] \le (1 - \varepsilon/d)^{\mathsf{wt}(\alpha)} \le (1 - \varepsilon/d)^{\beta d} \le e^{-\beta \varepsilon} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

since  $\beta \varepsilon = \omega(\log n)$ . Thus with overwhelming probability over the choice of **e**, there exists at least one index i where  $e_i \neq 0$  and  $\alpha_i \neq 0$ . In this case, the polynomial g is a nonzero polynomial in  $e_i$ , and since  $e_i$  is sampled uniformly over  $\mathbb{F}_p$ , we apply Schwartz-Zippel to conclude that the probability that  $g(\mathbf{r}, s, \mathbf{e}) = 0$  is  $1/|\mathbb{F}_p| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

- Suppose that  $\mathbf{H}\alpha \neq \mathbf{0}$ . Then,  $g(\mathbf{r}, s, \mathbf{e})$  is a nonzero polynomial in  $\mathbf{r}$ , and since  $\mathbf{r} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^{\ell}$ , by Schwartz-Zippel, the probability that  $g(\mathbf{r}, s, \mathbf{e}) = 0$  is  $1/|\mathbb{F}_p| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

We conclude that if the adversary queries GGM. Test on a polynomial that is not identically-zero in the formal variables  $\hat{z}_1, \ldots, \hat{z}_k, \hat{s}$ , then the output of GGM. Test in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i-1}$  will be "nonzero" with overwhelming probability, which matches the behavior in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{0,i}$ .

The claim now follows by a hybrid argument over the adversary's queries.

**Lemma 5.22.** For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  making a polynomial number of queries to  $\mathcal{G}$ ,  $|\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A}) = 1] - \Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A}) = 1]| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

*Proof.* The only difference between hybrids  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  is that the challenger defers the sampling of st until the very end. Certainly, the challenge  $((\mathsf{pp}, p, g), \mathbf{c}, h_1, \ldots, h_k)$  are identically distributed in the two experiments (they do not depend on st). We take a similar query-by-query approach where st is sampled after the first i queries of the adversary. To show that  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$  are statistically indistinguishable, it suffices to show that the response to  $i^{\text{th}}$  query is statistically indistinguishable in the two experiments. Since the GGM.Setup and GGM.Encode are handled identically in the two experiments, it suffices to consider GGM.Add and GGM.Test queries:

- Suppose the  $i^{\text{th}}$  query is a GGM.Add query on inputs  $k, \xi_1, \xi_2$ . The behavior in the two experiments are identical unless  $\xi_1 = \text{st}$  or  $\xi_2 = \text{st}$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$ . Since st is sampled after the first i-1 queries, that means that st is sampled independently of  $\xi_1$  and  $\xi_2$ , so over the randomness of st, the probability that  $\mathsf{st} = \xi_1$  or  $\mathsf{st} = \xi_2$  is  $2/2^{\lambda} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .
- Suppose the  $i^{\text{th}}$  query is a GGM. Test query on inputs  $k, \xi$ . By the same argument as in the previous case, the behavior is identical unless  $\xi = \text{st}$  where st is sampled uniformly and independently of  $\xi$ . This happens with probability  $1/2^{\lambda} = \text{negl}(\lambda)$  and the claim holds.

The claim now follows by a standard hybrid argument over the adversary's oracle queries.  $\Box$ 

<span id="page-40-0"></span>**Lemma 5.23.** For all adversaries A,  $Pr[Hyb_2(A) = 1] = negl(\lambda)$ .

*Proof.* Let  $\pi \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  be the adversary's output in this experiment. After the adversary outputs  $\pi$ , the challenger samples  $\mathsf{st} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , and with probability  $1-2^{-\lambda}$ ,  $\pi \neq \mathsf{st}$ . Then, there are two possibilities. If  $\pi \notin \mathsf{T}$ , then the output of the experiment is 0. If  $\pi \in \mathsf{T}$ , it maps to a polynomial that is independent of  $\hat{s}$  (since the only mapping  $\mathsf{st} \mapsto \hat{s}$  that involves the formal variable  $\hat{s}$  is added *after* the adversary has output  $\pi$ ). In this case,  $\pi$  and  $\mathsf{st}$  cannot correspond to identical polynomials, and the experiment outputs 0.

The claim now follows by combining Lemmas 5.21 to 5.23.

{41}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-45-14"></span>A Succinct Non-Interactive Arguments and Laconic Arguments

We recall the definitions of a succinct non-interactive argument (SNARG) and a two-message laconic argument for arithmetic circuit satisfiability (which includes Boolean circuit satisfiability as a special case).

<span id="page-45-16"></span>Definition A.1 (Succinct Non-Interactive Argument). Let C = {C`}`∈<sup>N</sup> be a family of arithmetic circuits and let R<sup>C</sup> be the corresponding circuit satisfiability relation (and let L<sup>C</sup> be the associated language). A succinct non-interactive argument (SNARG) for R<sup>C</sup> with completeness error c = c(λ) and soundness error ε = ε(λ) is a tuple ΠSNARG = (SSNARG,PSNARG, VSNARG) with the following syntax:

 SSNARG(1<sup>λ</sup> ) → (crs,st): On input the security parameter λ, the setup algorithm outputs a common reference string crs and verification state st.

{46}------------------------------------------------

- PSNARG(crs, x, w) → π: On input a common reference string crs, a statement x, and a witness w, the prove algorithm outputs a proof π.
- VSNARG(st, x, π) → {0, 1}: On input the verification state st, a statement x and a proof π, the verification algorithm outputs a bit b ∈ {0, 1}.

Moreover, ΠSNARG should satisfy the following properties:

Completeness: For all λ ∈ N and all (x, w) ∈ RC,

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(\mathsf{st}, x, \pi) = 1 \mid (\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(1^{\lambda}), \pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(\mathsf{crs}, x, w)] \geq 1 - c(\lambda).$$

When c(λ) = negl(λ), we say that ΠSNARG provides statistical completeness and when c(λ) = 0, we say that ΠSNARG provides perfect completeness.

 Soundness: We say that ΠSNARG is non-adaptively sound if for all λ ∈ N, and all efficient provers P ∗ , and all instances x /∈ LC,

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(\mathsf{st}, x, \pi) = 1 \mid (\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(1^{\lambda}), \pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}^*(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{crs}, x)] \leq \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

We say that ΠSNARG is adaptively sound if for all λ ∈ N, and all efficient provers P ∗ , we have that

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(\mathsf{st}, x, \pi) = 1 \land x \notin \mathcal{L}_{\mathcal{C}} \mid (\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(1^{\lambda}), (x, \pi) \leftarrow \mathcal{P}^*(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{crs})] \leq \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

 Optimal preprocessing succinctness: There exists universal polynomials p1, p<sup>2</sup> (independent of C) such that SSNARG and PSNARG run in time p1(λ, |C`|), VSNARG runs in time o(|C`|) · p2(λ, |x|), and the proof size is O(λ).

Remark A.2 (Polylogarithmic Verification Complexity). Some definitions of succinct arguments impose a more stringent efficiency requirement that the verification complexity is polylogarithmic in the size |C`| of the classic NP verifier. Our focus in this work is minimizing the proof size, so our only requirement on the verification complexity is that it should be sublinear in the size of the classic NP verifier (similar to the original definition from [\[GW11\]](#page-44-3)).

Remark A.3 (Public vs. Designated Verifier). We say a SNARG is publicly-verifiable if the verification state st = ⊥ (namely, anyone can verify in this case). We say a SNARG is in the designated-verifier model if only the holder of st is able to verify proofs (i.e., VSNARG takes st as input).

2-message laconic arguments. A 2-message laconic argument can be viewed as a SNARG with a "statement-dependent" common reference string which the verifier sends to the prover in the first round. The remaining properties are defined analogously to Definition [A.1.](#page-45-16) We give the full definition below:

Definition A.4 (2-Message Laconic Argument). Let C = {C`}`∈<sup>N</sup> be a family of arithmetic circuits, and let R<sup>C</sup> be the corresponding circuit satisfiability relation (and let L<sup>C</sup> be the associated language). A 2 message laconic argument for R<sup>C</sup> with completeness error c = c(λ) and soundness error ε = ε(λ) is a tuple ΠLA = (QLA,PLA, VLA) with the following syntax:

- QLA(1<sup>λ</sup> , x) → (q,st) : On input the security parameter λ and a statement x, the query algorithm outputs a query q and a verification state st.
- PLA(q, x, w) → π: On input a query q, a statement x, and a witness w, the prove algorithm outputs a proof π.
- VLA(st, π) → {0, 1}: On input the verification state st and a proof π, the verification algorithm outputs a bit b ∈ {0, 1}.

Moreover, ΠLA = (QLA,PLA, VLA) should satisfy the following properties:

{47}------------------------------------------------

• Completeness: For all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and all  $(x, w) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st},\pi) = 1 \mid (q,\mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda},x), \pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}(q,x,w)] \geq 1 - c(\lambda).$$

If  $c(\lambda) = \text{negl}(\lambda)$ , then we say  $\Pi_{LA}$  is statistically correct and if  $c(\lambda) = 0$ , then we say that  $\Pi_{LA}$  is perfectly correct.

• Soundness: For all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and all efficient provers  $\mathcal{P}^*$ , and all instances  $x \notin \mathcal{L}_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}(\mathsf{st},\pi) = 1 \mid (q,\mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}(1^{\lambda},x), \pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}^*(1^{\lambda},q,x)] \leq \varepsilon(\lambda).$$

• Optimal succinctness: There exist universal polynomials  $p_1, p_2$  (independent of C) such that  $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  and  $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  run in time  $p_1(\lambda, |C_\ell|)$ ,  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  runs in time  $o(|C_\ell|) \cdot p_2(\lambda, |x|)$  and the length of the proof output by  $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LA}}$  is  $O(\lambda)$ .

### <span id="page-47-0"></span>A.1 The Bitansky et al. Compiler

In this section, we recall the compiler of Bitansky et al. [BCI<sup>+</sup>13] from linear PCPs to preprocessing SNARGs. The general compiler proceeds in two main steps:

- The first step of the compiler constructs a 2-message linear interactive proof (LIP) from a linear PCP. At a high level, a linear interactive proof is a standard interactive proof [GMR85], except that each message from the prover to the verifier is a linear (or more generally, affine) function of the verifier's messages. We refer to  $[BCI^+13, \S 2.3]$  for the formal definition. Bitansky et al. showed how to compile any k-query linear PCP into a 2-message LIP by introducing a linear consistency check.
- The second step of the compiler takes a 2-message LIP and constructs from it a preprocessing SNARG using a notion called linear-only encryption (i.e., an encryption scheme that only supports linear homomorphism). The approach here is to encrypt the verifier's query in the LIP using the linear-only encryption scheme, and have the prover homomorphically compute its response to the encrypted queries. Here, the linear-only property constrains the prover to only implement linear (or more generally, affine) strategies, thus cryptographically enforcing the constraints of the LIP model. This step can also be instantiated from weaker notions of linear-only such as linear targeted malleability [BSW12]. In the case where the verifier's message in the LIP is statement-independent, the verifier's encrypted queries can be generated in advance (as part of the common reference string); this yields a preprocessing SNARG. Conversely, if the verifier's message in the LIP is statement-dependent, then the resulting construction yields a 2-message laconic argument. In Appendix C.1, we show that the ElGamal encryption scheme over a group G (see Construction C.4) satisfies the necessary notion of linear targeted malleability to instantiate the [BCI<sup>+</sup>13] compiler when the group G is modeled as a generic group.

As noted in [BCI<sup>+</sup>13, Remark 2.8], a linear PCP with one query is already a 2-message LIP, where the prover's message consists of a single field element. Thus, we can directly apply the second step of the [BCI<sup>+</sup>13] compiler to a 1-query linear PCP without needing to first construct a linear interactive proof. Below, we give the formal statements of the [BCI<sup>+</sup>13] compiler starting from 1-query linear PCPs (as this is the only setting we require in this work). We start by recalling the definition of linear targeted malleability.

<span id="page-47-1"></span>**Definition A.5** (Additively Homomorphic Encryption). An additively homomorphic encryption scheme over a ring  $\mathbb{Z}_q$  is a tuple of algorithms  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}} = (\mathsf{Setup}, \mathsf{Encrypt}, \mathsf{Decrypt}, \mathsf{Add}, \mathsf{ImVer})$  with the following syntax:

- Setup( $1^{\lambda}$ )  $\rightarrow$  (pk, sk): On input the security parameter  $\lambda$ , the setup algorithm outputs a public key pk and a secret key sk.
- Encrypt(pk, m)  $\to$  ct: On input the public key pk and a message  $m \in \mathbb{Z}_q$ , the encryption algorithm outputs a ciphertext ct.

{48}------------------------------------------------

- Decrypt(sk, ct)  $\to m$ : On input the secret key sk and a ciphertext ct, the decryption algorithm outputs a message  $m \in \mathbb{Z}_q \cup \{\bot\}$ .
- $Add(pk, ct_1, ct_2) \rightarrow ct'$ : On input the public key pk and two ciphertexts  $ct_1, ct_2$ , the addition algorithm outputs a new ciphertext ct'.
- $ImVer(sk, ct) \rightarrow \{0, 1\}$ : On input the secret key sk and a ciphertext ct, the image-verification algorithm outputs a bit  $b \in \{0, 1\}$ .

Moreover, an additively homomorphic encryption scheme should satisfy the following properties:

• Correctness: For every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and every message  $m \in \mathbb{Z}_q$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Decrypt}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}) = m \land \mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}) = 1 \, \middle| \, \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda); \\ \mathsf{ct} \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk},m) \end{array} \right] = 1.$$

• Additive homomorphism: For every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , every  $(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk})$  in the support of  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ , and all ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2$  in the support of  $\mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, m_1)$  and  $\mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, m_2)$ , respectively, where  $m_1, m_2 \in \mathbb{Z}_q$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Decrypt}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}') = m_1 + m_2 \land \mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}') = 1 \mid \mathsf{ct}' \leftarrow \mathsf{Add}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{ct}_1,\mathsf{ct}_2)] = 1.$$

• Semantic security: For any (sufficiently large)  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , and every efficient and stateful adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ ,

$$\Pr\left[b = b' \middle| \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}), (m_0, m_1) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{pk}); \\ b \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}, \mathsf{ct}_b \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, m_b); \\ b' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{ct}_b) \end{array} \right] \leq \frac{1}{2} + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

In our case, we allow Decrypt and ImVer to additionally take as input a set  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{Z}_q$  of potential messages, and we only require the first two properties to hold for ciphertexts encrypting messages in  $\mathcal{M}$ :

• Correctness: For every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , every set  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{Z}_q$ , and every message  $m \in \mathcal{M}$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Decrypt}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct},\mathcal{M}) = m \land \mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct},\mathcal{M}) = 1 \, \middle| \, \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda); \\ \mathsf{ct} \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk},m) \end{array} \right] = 1.$$

• Additive homomorphism: For every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , every set  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{Z}_q$ , every  $(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk})$  in the support of  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda)$ , and every ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2$  in the support of  $\mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, m_1)$  and  $\mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, m_2)$ , respectively, where  $m_1, m_2 \in \mathbb{Z}_q$ , if  $m_1 + m_2 \in \mathcal{M}$ , then

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Decrypt}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}',\mathcal{M}) = m_1 + m_2 \land \mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}',\mathcal{M}) = 1 \mid \mathsf{ct}' \leftarrow \mathsf{Add}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{ct}_1,\mathsf{ct}_2)] = 1.$$

<span id="page-48-0"></span>**Definition A.6** (Linear Targeted Malleability [BSW12, adapted]). An additively homomorphic encryption scheme  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}} = (\mathsf{Setup}, \mathsf{Encrypt}, \mathsf{Decrypt}, \mathsf{Add}, \mathsf{ImVer})$  over  $\mathbb{Z}_q$  satisfies linear targeted malleability if for every efficient adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  and plaintext generator  $\mathcal{T}$ , there is an efficient simulator  $\mathcal{S}$  such that for any sufficiently large  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and auxiliary input  $z \in \{0,1\}^{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}$ , the outputs of the following two distributions are computationally indistinguishable:

#### Real Distribution:

- 1.  $(pk, sk) \leftarrow Setup(1^{\lambda})$
- 2.  $(s, a_1, \ldots, a_m) \leftarrow \mathcal{T}(\mathsf{pk})$
- 3.  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, a_i) \text{ for all } i \in [m]$
- 4.  $\mathsf{ct}_1', \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_k' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{ct}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_m; z)$
- 5.  $a_i' \leftarrow \mathsf{Decrypt}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}') \text{ for all } i \in [k]$
- 6. If  $\mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{ct}_i') = 1$  for all  $i \in [k]$ , output  $(\mathsf{pk}, \{a_i\}_{i \in [m]}, s, \{a_i'\}_{i \in [k]})$ .
- 7. Otherwise, output  $\perp$ .

#### Ideal Distribution:

- 1.  $(pk, sk) \leftarrow Setup(1^{\lambda})$
- 2.  $(s, a_1, \ldots, a_m) \leftarrow \mathcal{T}(\mathsf{pk})$
- 3.  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, a_i) \text{ for all } i \in [m]$
- 4.  $\operatorname{ct}_1', \ldots, \operatorname{ct}_k' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\operatorname{pk}, \operatorname{ct}_1, \ldots, \operatorname{ct}_m; z)$
- 5.  $(\mathbf{\Pi}, \mathbf{b}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}(\mathsf{pk}; z)$
- 6.  $(a_1', \ldots, a_k')^\mathsf{T} \leftarrow \mathbf{\Pi} \cdot (a_1, \ldots, a_m)^\mathsf{T} + \mathbf{b}$
- 7. If  $ImVer(ct'_i) = 1$  for all  $i \in [k]$ , output  $(pk, \{a_i\}_{i \in [m]}, s, \{a'_i\}_{i \in [k]})$ .
- 8. Otherwise, output  $\perp$ .

{49}------------------------------------------------

In our setting, we will also consider a restricted version of linear targeted malleability where we require the above distributions to be indistinguishable only when the ciphertexts produced by the adversary correspond to messages drawn from a restricted subset  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{Z}_q$ . In this case, we augment the above definition by also including the set  $\mathcal{M}$  as an input to Decrypt and ImVer (Definition A.5). In this case, we say that  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  satisfies linear targeted malleability with respect to the target message space  $\mathcal{M}$ .

Remark A.7 (Auxiliary Input Distributions). Definition A.6 requires the simulator to succeed for arbitrary auxiliary inputs  $z \in \{0,1\}^{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}$ . This requirement is quite strong since z can be used to encode difficult cryptographic problems that the simulator needs to solve in order to correctly simulate the output distribution [BCPR14]. However, in many scenarios, it suffices to just consider "benign" distributions for which the definition plausibly holds. For instance, in our application to preprocessing SNARGs and laconic arguments, it suffices to consider the setting where the auxiliary input z is a uniform string.

**Preprocessing SNARGs from linear PCPs.** We now recall the construction of preprocessing SNARGs from any 1-query linear PCP in conjunction with an additively homomorphic encryption scheme satisfying linear targeted malleability.

<span id="page-49-2"></span>Construction A.8 (SNARG from 1-Query Linear PCP [BCI<sup>+</sup>13]). Let  $\mathcal{C} = \{C_\ell\}_{\ell \in \mathbb{N}}$  be a family of arithmetic circuits over a finite field  $\mathbb{F}$  and let  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$  be the associated circuit satisfiability relation. Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}})$  be a 1-query input-oblivious linear PCP for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$  over  $\mathbb{F}$ , and let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}} = (\mathsf{Setup}, \mathsf{Encrypt}, \mathsf{Decrypt}, \mathsf{Add}, \mathsf{ImVer})$  be an additively homomorphic encryption scheme over  $\mathbb{F}$ . We construct a SNARG  $\Pi_{\mathsf{SNARG}} = (\mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}, \mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{SNARG}})$  for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$  as follows:

- $S_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(1^{\lambda})$ : On input the security parameter  $\lambda$ , the setup algorithm runs  $(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$  and  $(\mathsf{st}', \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{q}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  where  $\mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}} \in \mathbb{F}^n$  and  $\mathbf{q} = (q_1, \ldots, q_\ell) \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$ . Then, the setup algorithm computes the ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, q_i)$  for all  $i \in [\ell]$ . The setup algorithm outputs the CRS  $\mathsf{crs} = (\mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{ct}_i\}_{i \in [\ell]})$  and the secret verification state  $\mathsf{st} = (\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{st}', \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}})$ .
- $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(\mathsf{crs}, \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w})$ : On input a common reference string  $\mathsf{crs} = (\mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{ct}_i\}_{i \in [\ell]})$ , a statement  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{F}^n$ , and a witness  $\mathbf{w}$ , the prover computes a proof  $\pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}) \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$ . It interprets  $\{\mathsf{ct}_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  as the encryption of a vector  $\mathbf{q} \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$  and homomorphically computes an encryption  $\mathsf{ct'}$  of the value  $\mathbf{q}^\mathsf{T}\pi$ . It outputs the proof  $\pi = \mathsf{ct'}$ .
- $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{SNARG}}(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{x}, \pi)$ : On input the verification state  $\mathsf{st} = (\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{st}', \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}})$ , the statement  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{F}^n$ , and a proof  $\pi = \mathsf{ct}'$ , the verifier first checks that  $\mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}') = 1$  and outputs 0 if not. Otherwise, it computes  $a \leftarrow \mathsf{Decrypt}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}')$  and outputs  $\mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathsf{st}', \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}, a)$ .

<span id="page-49-0"></span>**Theorem A.9** (Preprocessing SNARG from 1-Query Linear PCP [BCI<sup>+</sup>13], adapted). If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  has soundness error  $\varepsilon$  and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  satisfies linear targeted malleability, then Construction A.8 gives a preprocessing SNARG for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$  with perfect completeness and (non-adaptive) soundness error  $\varepsilon + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-49-1"></span>Remark A.10 (Adaptive Soundness). Previously, Bitansky et al. [BCI<sup>+</sup>13] showed that if the underlying encryption scheme  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  in Construction A.8 satisfies the stronger extractability notion of linear-only security, then the resulting SNARG satisfies adaptive soundness (where the malicious prover can choose the statement to prove after seeing the CRS). In addition, we note that some specific instantiations of Construction A.8 can also be shown to satisfy adaptive soundness without necessarily requiring  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  satisfy the linear-only property. For example, we show in Appendix C.2 that Construction A.8 provides adaptive soundness when  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  is instantiated with the ElGamal encryption scheme over a group  $\mathbb G$  that is modeled as a generic group. In this setting of adaptively-sound SNARGs, the Gentry-Wichs lower bound [GW11] rules out the possibility of basing security on a non-falsifiable assumption. As such, some kind of non-falsifiable assumption (e.g., the linear-only assumption) or working in an idealized model like the generic group model seems necessary for the security analysis.

{50}------------------------------------------------

**Laconic arguments from linear PCPs.** If the underlying linear PCP in Construction A.8 is *input-dependent*, then we obtain a SNARG where the CRS is statement-dependent—that is, a 2-message laconic argument. Thus, we can state the corresponding analog of Theorem A.9 for laconic arguments:

<span id="page-50-2"></span>**Theorem A.11** (Laconic Arguments from 1-Query Linear PCPs [BCI<sup>+</sup>13, adapted]). If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  is an inputdependent linear PCP with soundness error  $\varepsilon$  and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  satisfies linear targeted malleability, then Construction A.8 yields a 2-message laconic argument for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$  with perfect completeness and soundness error  $\varepsilon + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-50-3"></span>Remark A.12 (Bounded Linear PCPs). When a 1-query linear PCP has the property that all honestly-generated proofs lie in a set  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{F}$  known to the verifier (e.g.,  $\mathcal{M}$  may be determined by the query-generation algorithm), we can tweak Construction A.8 so that Theorems A.9 and A.11 hold as long the encryption scheme  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  in Construction A.8 satisfy linear targeted malleability with respect to  $\mathcal{M}$  (Definition A.6). Namely, we modify the verification algorithm to additionally include the set  $\mathcal{M}$  as input to ImVer and Decrypt. This means that the SNARG verifier only accepts if the prover's response is an encryption of a message in the set  $\mathcal{M}$ , and moreover, every prover strategy that convinces the verifier to accept can be explained by an affine function of the verifier's encrypted queries. Thus, completeness and soundness follow by the same argument as in the proofs of Theorems A.9 and A.11 from [BCI+13]. An immediate consequence is that if we have a bounded linear PCP where honestly-generated responses lie in a polynomial-size subset  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq |\mathbb{F}|$ , we can obtain a SNARG (or laconic argument) from an encryption scheme that satisfies linearly targeted malleability with respect to polynomial-size subsets (e.g., the ElGamal encryption scheme (Construction C.4 and Theorem C.6)).

## <span id="page-50-0"></span>B Linear PCPs for Boolean Circuit Satisfiability

In this section, we review the classic construction of a linear PCP based on the Walsh-Hadamard code (following the presentation from [BCI<sup>+</sup>13, BISW17]). We then show how to modify it to obtain a bounded 2-query linear PCP with either strong soundness or  $\delta$ -honest verifier zero knowledge for the language of Boolean circuit satisfiability.

#### <span id="page-50-1"></span>B.1 A Linear PCP from the Hadamard Code

We begin by describing a 2-query linear PCP based on the Hadamard PCP from [ALM<sup>+</sup>98, IKO07], extended to work over arbitrary finite fields F. Our presentation follows that from [BCI<sup>+</sup>13, BISW17]. We begin by describing the 3-query variant from previous works [IKO07, BCI<sup>+</sup>13], and then show that a simple variant allows us to combine two of the queries, yielding a 2-query linear PCP.

Construction description. Let  $C: \mathbb{F}_p^n \times \mathbb{F}_p^h \to \mathbb{F}_p^t$  be an arithmetic circuit of size s over a finite field  $\mathbb{F}_p$ . Let  $z_i$  denote the value of the  $i^{\text{th}}$  wire of C on an input  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{F}_p^n$  and a witness  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{F}_p^h$ . The wires  $z_i$  for  $i \in [n]$  correspond to the values of the input wires, and the wires  $z_{s-i+1} = 0$  for  $i \in [t]$  correspond to the values of the output wires. An honestly generated proof consists of a vector  $\boldsymbol{\pi} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s+s^2}$  whose components are the values  $z_1, \ldots, z_s \in \mathbb{F}_p$  and the products  $z_i z_j \in \mathbb{F}_p$  for all  $i, j \in [s]$ . If we define  $\mathbf{z} = [z_1, \ldots, z_s]$ , then we can write  $\boldsymbol{\pi} = [\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}]$ . To verify the proof, the verifier performs the following four consistency checks:

- 1. Consistency of  $\pi$ : each entry  $z_i z_j$  is the product of the corresponding  $z_i$  and  $z_j$ .
- 2. Input consistency:  $z_i = x_i$  for all  $i \in [n]$ .
- 3. Output consistency:  $z_{s-i+1} = 0$  for all  $i \in [t]$ .
- 4. **Gate consistency:** For each gate, the value of the output wire is consistent with the value of its input wires. In particular, for addition gates with input wires i, j and output wire k, this corresponds to checking that  $z_i + z_j z_k = 0$ . For the multiplication gates with input wires i, j and output wire k, this corresponds to checking that  $z_i z_j z_k = 0$ .

{51}------------------------------------------------

These consistency checks can be verified as follows:

- To verify the first condition, the verifier requests a random linear combination of the  $z_i$ 's and a corresponding linear combination of the product terms  $z_i z_j$ 's whose sum corresponds to the square of the first query. Namely, the verifier samples  $\mathbf{v} \leftarrow \mathbb{F}_p^s$  and constructs query vectors  $\mathbf{q}_1 = [\mathbf{v}, 0^{s^2}]$  and  $\mathbf{q}_2 = [0^s, \mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{v}]$ . The verifier then checks the relation  $(\mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi})^2 \stackrel{?}{=} \mathbf{q}_2^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}$ . If  $\boldsymbol{\pi}$  is not of the form  $[\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}]$  for some  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{F}_p^s$ , then by the Schwartz-Zippel lemma,  $(\mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi})^2 = \mathbf{q}_2^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}$  with probability at most 2/p.
- The remaining consistency checks can be expressed as a linear system in terms of the variables  $\mathbf{z}$  and  $\mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}$ . Namely, we can define a matrix  $\mathbf{A}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s \times (s+s^2)}$  and a vector  $\mathbf{b}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s-n}$  where  $\mathbf{A}_C$  and  $\mathbf{b}_C$  are functions of C only (and which can be computed in linear time from C), such that  $\mathbf{z}$  is a satisfying assignment to the wires of C only if

$$\mathbf{A}_C \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{z} \\ \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z} \end{array} \right] = \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{x} \\ \mathbf{b}_C \end{array} \right].$$

To check that this system is satisfied, the verifier samples  $\mathbf{u} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^s$  and checks the relation

$$\mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{z} \ \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z} \end{array} \right] \stackrel{?}{=} \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{x} \ \mathbf{b}_C \end{array} \right] = \mathbf{u}_\mathbf{x}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x} + \mathbf{u}_\mathbf{b}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C$$

where  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}} \in \mathbb{F}_p^n$  denotes the first n components of  $\mathbf{u}$  and  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}$  denotes the other s-n components. Concretely, the verifier sets  $\mathbf{q}_3 \leftarrow \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C$  and checks the relation  $\mathbf{q}_3^\mathsf{T} \pi \stackrel{?}{=} \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x} + \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C$ . Assuming that  $\pi$  is consistent (i.e., of the form  $[\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}]$  for some  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{F}_p^s$ ), then by the Schwartz-Zippel lemma, the verifier will reject a inconsistent wire assignment with probability at least 1/p.

As described, the verifier prepares three queries  $\mathbf{q}_1, \mathbf{q}_2, \mathbf{q}_3$  and receives responses  $a_1 = \mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}$ ,  $a_2 = \mathbf{q}_2^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}$ , and  $a_3 = \mathbf{q}_3^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}$  and checks that  $a_1^2 \stackrel{?}{=} a_2$  and  $a_3 \stackrel{?}{=} \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x} + \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C$ . We make several observations:

- The decision procedure is fully linear and only depends on  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^{\mathsf{T}}, \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{b}_{C}$ .
