- The responses  $a_2$  and  $a_3$  are used in a linear manner at the decision procedure, and depend on independently-sampled randomness. Thus, instead of checking them independently, we can instead check the joint relation

<span id="page-51-1"></span>
$$a_1^2 - a_2 + (a_3 - \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x} - \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C) \stackrel{?}{=} 0.$$
 (B.1)

By the Schwartz-Zippel lemma, if either of the two verification relations does not hold, then Eq. (B.1) is nonzero with probability at least 2/p. The key is that we can check Eq. (B.1) using just two queries by setting  $\bar{\mathbf{q}}_1 = \mathbf{q}_1$  and  $\bar{\mathbf{q}}_2 = \mathbf{q}_3 - \mathbf{q}_2$  and testing  $\bar{a}_1^2 + \bar{a}_2 - \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{x} - \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{b}_C \stackrel{?}{=} 0$ , where  $\bar{a}_1 = \bar{\mathbf{q}}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \boldsymbol{\pi}$  and  $\bar{a}_2 = \bar{\mathbf{q}}_2^{\mathsf{T}} \boldsymbol{\pi}$ . This precisely corresponds to Eq. (B.1) above.

<span id="page-51-0"></span>We give the formal construction below:

Construction B.1 (2-Query Linear PCP from the Walsh-Hadamard Code). Let  $C: \mathbb{F}_p^n \times \mathbb{F}_p^h \to \mathbb{F}_p^t$  be an arithmetic circuit of size s over  $\mathbb{F}_p$ . We construct a 2-query linear PCP  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}})$  for  $\mathcal{R}_C$  as follows:

- $\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$ : Let  $\mathbf{A}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s \times (s+s^2)}$  and  $\mathbf{b}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s-n}$  be the circuit-specific linear system that correspond to the circuit consistency checks. The query algorithm then computes two query vectors as follows  $(\mathbf{q}_1, \mathbf{q}_2)$ :
  - 1. Sample  $\mathbf{v} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^s$ . Set  $\mathbf{q}_1^{\mathsf{T}} = [\mathbf{v}^{\mathsf{T}}, 0^{s^2}]$ .
  - 2. Sample  $\mathbf{u} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^s$ . Let  $\mathbf{q}_2^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{u}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A}_C [0^s, (\mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{v})^{\mathsf{T}}]$ , and  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}} \in \mathbb{F}_p^n$  be the first n components of  $\mathbf{u}$ , and  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s-n}$  be the rest of the components of  $\mathbf{u}$ .

{52}------------------------------------------------

The query sets  $\mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}} = \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}$  and  $\mathbf{Q} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{(s+s^2)\times 2}$  to be the matrix whose columns are  $\mathbf{q}_1$  and  $\mathbf{q}_2$ . It also computes  $u_C \leftarrow \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C$  and sets  $\mathsf{st} = u_C$ . Finally, it outputs  $\mathsf{st}$ ,  $\mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}$ , and  $\mathbf{Q}$ .

- $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w})$ : On input  $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}) \in \mathbb{F}_p^n \times \mathbb{F}_p^h$  where  $C(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}) = \mathbf{0}$ , the prover computes the values  $z_1, \ldots, z_s$  for the wires of C. It sets  $\mathbf{z} = [z_1, \ldots, z_s] \in \mathbb{F}_p^s$  and outputs the proof  $\boldsymbol{\pi} = [\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}]^\mathsf{T} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s+s^2}$ .
- $\mathcal{D}_{LPCP}(\mathsf{st}, a_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{a})$  On input the state  $\mathsf{st} = u_C$ , an input-dependent response  $a_{\mathsf{inp}}$ , and a vector of responses  $\mathbf{a} = (a_1, a_2) \in \mathbb{F}_p^2$ , the verifier outputs 1 if  $a_1^2 + a_2 = a_{\mathsf{inp}} + u_C$ , and 0 otherwise.

<span id="page-52-3"></span>**Theorem B.2** (Completeness). Construction B.1 satisfies perfect completeness.

Proof. Take any  $\mathbf{x}$ ,  $\mathbf{w}$  where  $C(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}) = 0^t$ . Let  $(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{Q}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  and  $\boldsymbol{\pi} \leftarrow \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w})$ . By construction,  $\boldsymbol{\pi} = [\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}]$ , where  $\mathbf{z}$  is the wire values corresponding to  $C(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w})$ . Let  $a_1 \leftarrow \mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}$  and  $a_2 \leftarrow \mathbf{q}_2^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}$ , where  $\mathbf{q}_1$  and  $\mathbf{q}_2$  are the columns of  $\mathbf{Q}$ . Then,

$$a_1^2 + a_2 = (\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z})^2 + \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C \boldsymbol{\pi} - (\mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{v})^\mathsf{T} (\mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}) = (\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z})^2 + \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C \boldsymbol{\pi} - (\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z}) (\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z}) = \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C \boldsymbol{\pi}.$$

By construction of  $\mathbf{A}_C$ , if  $\boldsymbol{\pi} = [\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}]^\mathsf{T}$  for a valid wire labeling, then

$$\mathbf{u}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A}_C \boldsymbol{\pi} = \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{x} + \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{b}_C = a_{\mathsf{inp}} + u_C.$$

<span id="page-52-2"></span>**Theorem B.3** (Strong Soundness). Construction B.1 has strong soundness error 2/p.

Proof. Take any statement  $\mathbf{x} \notin \mathcal{L}$  and consider any affine proof strategy  $\boldsymbol{\pi}^* = [\boldsymbol{\pi}_1^*, \boldsymbol{\pi}_2^*] \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s+s^2}$ , where  $\boldsymbol{\pi}_1^* \in \mathbb{F}_p^s$  and  $\boldsymbol{\pi}_2^* \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s^2}$  and  $\delta_1^*, \delta_2^* \in \mathbb{F}_p$ . Let  $(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{Q}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$ , where  $\mathsf{st} = u_C$  and  $\mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}} = \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}$ . Let  $\mathbf{q}_1, \mathbf{q}_2$  be the columns of  $\mathbf{Q}$ . Write  $\mathbf{q}_1^{\mathsf{T}} = [\mathbf{v}^{\mathsf{T}}, 0^{s^2}]$  and  $\mathbf{q}_2^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{u}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A}_C - [0^s, (\mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{v})^{\mathsf{T}}]$ . Consider  $a_1 \leftarrow \mathbf{q}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \boldsymbol{\pi} + \delta_1$  and  $a_2 \leftarrow \mathbf{q}_2^{\mathsf{T}} \boldsymbol{\pi} + \delta_2$ . The verifier accepts if

<span id="page-52-1"></span>
$$a_1^2 + a_2 - a_{\text{inp}} - u_C = (\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}_1^* + \delta_1^*)^2 + \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C \boldsymbol{\pi}^* - (\mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{v})^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi}_2^* + \delta_2^* - \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x} - \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C = 0.$$
 (B.2)

We consider two possibilities:

- Suppose that  $\pi_2^* \neq \pi_1^* \otimes \pi_1^*$ . Then Eq. (B.2) is not identically zero in the variables  $\mathbf{v}$ .
- Suppose that  $\boldsymbol{\pi}^* = [\mathbf{z}^*, \mathbf{z}^* \otimes \mathbf{z}^*]$  for some  $\mathbf{z}^* \in \mathbb{F}_p^s$ . Since  $\mathbf{x} \notin \mathcal{L}$ , there is no consistent labeling of the wires of C with input  $\mathbf{x}$  and output values  $0^t$  (i.e., no value  $\mathbf{z}^*$  can satisfy the input, output, and circuit consistency constraints). This means that  $\mathbf{A}_C \boldsymbol{\pi}^* \neq [\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{b}_C]^\mathsf{T}$ , and so  $\mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C \boldsymbol{\pi}^* \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x} \mathbf{u}_b^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C$  is not the identically zero polynomial in the variables  $\mathbf{u}$ . Thus, in this case, Eq. (B.2) is not identically zero in the variables  $\mathbf{u}$ .

In both cases, the verification relation Eq. (B.2) is not identically zero in either  $\mathbf{u}$  or  $\mathbf{v}$ . Since these variables are sampled uniformly from  $\mathbb{F}_p$ , and Eq. (B.2) is a polynomial of total degree 2, we appeal to Schwartz-Zippel to conclude that over the verifier's choice of randomness, Eq. (B.2) holds with probability at most 2/p, and soundness follows. We can apply the same analysis as above to argue strong soundness (with the same soundness error).

**Remark B.4** (Reducing Query Length). We note that in the Hadamard LPCP from Construction B.1, the proof  $\pi$  has the form  $\pi = [\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}]$ . By default,  $\mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}$  contains both the value of  $z_i z_j$  as well as the value of  $z_j z_i$  whenever  $i \neq j$ . We can reduce the query size slightly by only including *one* copy of each pairwise product. This allows us to reduce the query length from  $s + s^2$  to  $s + s + s(s - 1)/2 = (s^2 + 3s)/2$ .

<span id="page-52-4"></span><span id="page-52-0"></span>**Remark B.5** (Honest-Verifier Zero Knowledge). As noted in [BCI<sup>+</sup>13, Remark A.4], it is straightforward to obtain a perfect honest-verifier zero-knowledge linear PCP from the Hadamard PCP by introducing a dummy wire to the circuit and having the prover assigning the wire a *random* value. The verifier then performs all of the checks with respect to this new circuit. We refer to [BCI<sup>+</sup>13, Remark A.4] for more details.

{53}------------------------------------------------

Remark B.6 (Knowledge Soundness). We further note that Construction B.1 satisfies knowledge soundness (Definition 2.7). From Theorem B.3 it is clear that any linear strategy  $\pi^*$ ,  $\delta^*$  that convinces  $\mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  with probability greater than  $\varepsilon$  must encode a satisfying assignment to the circuit C (with  $\delta^* = \mathbf{0}$ ). Therefore, the extractor  $\mathcal{E}$  can simply query  $\pi^*$  with  $|\mathbf{w}|$  unit vectors to recover the witness  $\mathbf{w}$ .

Corollary B.7 (2-Query Hadamard Linear PCP). Let  $C: \mathbb{F}_p^n \times \mathbb{F}_p^h \to \mathbb{F}_p^t$  be an arithmetic circuit of size s over  $\mathbb{F}_p$ . Then there exists a 2-query, degree-2 linear PCP with perfect zero-knowledge for the relation  $\mathcal{R}_C$  with query length  $t = (s^2 + 3s)/2 = O(s^2)$  and strong soundness error 2/p.

#### <span id="page-53-1"></span>B.2 Bounded Linear PCP from the Hadamard Code

It is straightforward to adapt the Hadamard linear PCP from Construction B.1 to obtain a bounded linear PCP. Here, we will focus on the particular setting of Boolean circuits, so the input and output wires of the circuit are *binary*. Our construction naturally generalizes to general circuits over the integers with bounded wire values (provided that each gate in the circuit computes a quadratic function of its inputs). Below, we present our construction for Boolean circuits:

<span id="page-53-0"></span>Construction B.8 (2-Query Bounded Linear PCP). Let  $C: \{0,1\}^n \times \{0,1\}^h \to \{0,1\}$  be a Boolean circuit of size s. Let  $b_1, b_2: \mathbb{N} \to \mathbb{N}$  be bound functions. We construct a 2-query bounded linear PCP  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}})$  over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  for the relation  $\mathcal{R}_C$  as in Construction B.1, but with the following differences:

- Circuit consistency checks: In Construction B.1, we defined circuit consistency checks for arithmetic circuits over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  where the gates were addition and multiplication gates over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  and the wire values were arbitrary  $\mathbb{F}_p$  elements. Here, we work with Boolean circuits so that in the honest execution, all of the wire values are binary valued. In Table 3, we describe how to implement linear consistency checks for several standard Boolean gates. We assume that each gates has exactly two input wires. Using the same procedure as Construction B.1, we then define the circuit consistency check  $\mathbf{A}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s \times (s+s^2)}$  and  $\mathbf{b}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s-n}$  for a Boolean circuit C. Note that we include the same input-consistency and output-consistency checks as before.
- Bounded query domain: The query-generation algorithm  $\mathcal{Q}_{LPCP}$  now takes an additional bound parameter  $\tau \in \mathbb{N}$  and instead of sampling  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}$  from the full domain, it instead samples  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v} \leftarrow [-\tau/2, \tau/2]^s$ . The verification state st also includes the bound parameter  $\tau$ .
- Bound checking at verification time: On input st =  $(u_C, \tau)$ , an input-dependent response  $a_{\text{inp}}$ , and a vector of responses  $\mathbf{a} = (a_1, a_2) \in \mathbb{F}_p^2$ , the verifier additionally checks that  $a_1 \in [-b_1(\tau), b_1(\tau)]$  and  $a_2 \in [-b_2(\tau), b_2(\tau)]$ .

Everything else is constructed as in Construction B.1.

<span id="page-53-2"></span>**Theorem B.9** (Completeness). Construction B.8 satisfies perfect completeness.

*Proof.* Same as the proof of Theorem B.2.

<span id="page-53-3"></span>**Theorem B.10** (Soundness). Construction B.8 has strong soundness error  $2/\tau$ .

*Proof.* Follows from the same argument as in the proof of Theorem B.3. The only difference is that  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}$  are now sampled uniformly from an interval of size  $\tau$  rather than  $|\mathbb{F}_p|$ .

**Remark B.11** (Knowledge Soundness). Construction B.8 has  $2/\tau$  knowledge soundness (Definition 2.7). The argument is identical to the argument in Remark B.6

<span id="page-53-4"></span>**Theorem B.12** (Bounded). Construction B.8 is a bounded linear PCP where  $b_1(\tau) = s\tau/2$  and  $b_2(\tau) = 2(b_1(\tau))^2$ . Moreover, for any  $\varepsilon > 0$ , Construction B.8 is bounded with respect to bound functions  $b'_1(\tau) = \tau\sqrt{s/2 \cdot \ln(2/\varepsilon)}$  and  $b'_2(\tau) = 2(b'_1(\tau))^2$  with probability at least  $1 - \varepsilon$ .

{54}------------------------------------------------

| Gate Type | Input Wires | Output Wire | Linear Checks                   |
|-----------|-------------|-------------|---------------------------------|
| AND       | $z_i,z_j$   | $z_k$       | $z_k - z_{ij} = 0$              |
| NOT       | $z_i$       | $z_k$       | $z_i + z_k = 1$                 |
| OR        | $z_i,z_j$   | $z_k$       | $z_k - z_i - z_j + z_{ij} = 0$  |
| XOR       | $z_i,z_j$   | $z_k$       | $z_k - z_i - z_j + 2z_{ij} = 0$ |
| NAND      | $z_i,z_j$   | $z_k$       | $z_k + z_{ij} = 1$              |

<span id="page-54-2"></span>Table 3: Linear checks for common Boolean gates. We assume that each gate has two input wires and a single output wire. It is straightforward to extend this approach to arbitrary Boolean gates whose output value can be expressed as a quadratic function of its input values. Recall that the Hadamard linear PCP consists of a vector of wire labels  $\mathbf{z}$  together with their pairwise products  $\mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}$ . We write  $z_i$  to denote the  $i^{\text{th}}$  wire label in  $\mathbf{z}$  and  $z_{ij}$  to denote the entry in  $\mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}$  that corresponds to the product  $z_i z_j$ .

Proof. Take any  $x \in \{0,1\}^n$  and  $w \in \{0,1\}^h$  where C(x,w) = 1. Let  $(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{Q}) \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\tau)$  and  $\pi \leftarrow \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(x,w)$  where  $\mathsf{st} = (u_C,\tau)$ . By construction,  $\pi = [\mathbf{z},\mathbf{z}\otimes\mathbf{z}]$ , where  $\mathbf{z}\in\mathbb{F}_p^s$  are the wire labels of circuit C on input (x,w). Since C is a Boolean circuit, this means that every value in  $\pi$  is binary-valued. Next, let  $\mathbf{q}_1, \mathbf{q}_2$  denote the two columns of  $\mathbf{Q}$ . By construction  $\mathbf{q}_1$  contains exactly s nonzero entries, each of which is drawn from the interval  $[-\tau/2, \tau/2]$ . Thus,  $\mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} \pi \in [-s\tau/2, s\tau/2]$ . By completeness, we know that the verification relation is satisfied, in which case

$$\mathbf{q}_2^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi} = \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x} + u_C - (\mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi})^2.$$

Here  $\mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{x}$  and  $u_C = \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^{\mathsf{T}}b_C$ , where each component of  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}$  and  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}$  are sampled from the interval  $[-\tau/2, \tau/2]$ . For the Boolean gates in Table 3, we have that each component of  $\mathbf{b}_C$  is binary-valued, as is the input  $\mathbf{x}$ . Thus, if we work over the integers, then we can bound

$$|\mathbf{q}_{1}^{\mathsf{T}}\boldsymbol{\pi}| \leq |\mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{x}| + |u_{C}| + |\mathbf{q}_{1}^{\mathsf{T}}\boldsymbol{\pi}|^{2} \leq s\tau + (b_{1}(\tau))^{2} = 2b_{1}(\tau) + (b_{1}(\tau))^{2} \leq 2(b_{1}(\tau))^{2}.$$

Thus, Construction B.8 is bounded with respect to  $b_1, b_2$ . For the second part of the claim, it suffices to bound the value of  $\mathbf{q}_1^{\mathsf{T}}\boldsymbol{\pi}$ . This follows by a straightforward application of Hoeffding's inequality. Namely, for any bound B > 0, and working over the integers, we have

$$\Pr[|\mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T}\boldsymbol{\pi}| \ge B] \le 2\exp\left(-\frac{2B^2}{\mathsf{wt}(\mathbf{q}_1) \cdot \tau^2}\right) \le 2\exp\left(-\frac{2B^2}{s\tau^2}\right).$$

Substituting  $B = b'_1(\tau)$ , we have

$$\Pr[|\mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T}\boldsymbol{\pi}| \geq b_1'(\tau)] \leq 2\exp\left(-\frac{2\tau^2s/2\cdot\ln(2/\varepsilon)}{s\tau^2}\right) = \varepsilon.$$

The claim then follows by a similar argument as before.

#### <span id="page-54-0"></span>B.3 Zero-Knowledge via Noise Smudging

Our bounded linear PCP from Construction B.8 does not provide zero-knowledge (even against honest verifiers). Using the same idea from [BCI<sup>+</sup>13] (see Remark B.5), we can adapt Construction B.8 to satisfy  $\delta$ -HVZK while remaining a bounded linear PCP. Namely, as in [BCI<sup>+</sup>13], we introduce a dummy wire to the circuit, and the prover assigns a random value (chosen from a small interval) when constructing the proof. For completeness, we provide the full construction below:

<span id="page-54-1"></span>Construction B.13 (2-Query Bounded Linear PCP with Zero-Knowledge). Fix a parameter  $\delta > 0$ . Let  $b_1, b_2$  be bound functions (that depend on both the bound parameter  $\tau$  and the zero-knowledge parameter  $\delta$ ). Let  $C: \{0,1\}^n \times \{0,1\}^h \to \{0,1\}$  be a Boolean circuit of size s. We construct a 2-query bounded linear PCP  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}} = (\mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}, \mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}})$  over a finite field  $\mathbb{F}_p$  as follows:

{55}------------------------------------------------

- $Q_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\tau)$ : On input the bound parameter  $\tau$ , the query-generation algorithm samples  $\mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}' \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} [-\tau/2, \tau/2]^s$ , and defines  $\mathbf{v} = [(\mathbf{v}')^\mathsf{T}, 1]^\mathsf{T} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s+1}$ . Let  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}} \in \mathbb{F}_p^n$  be the first n components of  $\mathbf{u}$  and  $\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s-n}$  be the remaining s-n components of  $\mathbf{u}$ . The query algorithm constructs the circuit consistency check matrix  $\mathbf{A}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s \times ((s+1)+(s+1)^2)}$  and vector  $\mathbf{b}_C \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s-n}$  as in Construction B.8. It defines the queries  $\mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} = [\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T}, 0^{(s+1)^2}] \in \mathbb{F}_p^{(s+1)+(s+1)^2}$  and  $\mathbf{q}_2^\mathsf{T} = \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A}_C [0^{s+1}, (\mathbf{v} \otimes \mathbf{v})^\mathsf{T}] \in \mathbb{F}_p^{(s+1)+(s+1)^2}$ . Let  $\mathbf{Q} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{((s+1)+(s+1)^2) \times 2}$  be the matrix whose columns are  $\mathbf{q}_1$  and  $\mathbf{q}_2$ . It computes  $u_C \leftarrow \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{b}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_C$ , and outputs  $\mathbf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}} = \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{x}}$ , and  $\mathbf{Q}$ .
- $\mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\tau, x, w)$ : On input  $(x, w) \in \{0, 1\}^n \times \{0, 1\}^h$ , the prover evaluates C(x, w) and sets  $z_1, \ldots, z_s$  to be the wire values for C. Let  $B = 2\tau \sqrt{s/2 \cdot \ln(4/\delta)}/\delta$ . The prover samples a "smudging factor"  $z_{s+1} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} [-B, B]$ . Let  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{s+1}$  be the vector whose elements are  $z_1, \ldots, z_s, z_{s+1}$ . The prover outputs the proof  $\boldsymbol{\pi} = [\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z} \otimes \mathbf{z}] \in \mathbb{F}_p^{(s+1)+(s+1)^2}$ .
- $\mathcal{D}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathsf{st}, a_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{a})$ : On input the verification state  $\mathsf{st} = (\tau, u_C)$ , an input-dependent response  $a_{\mathsf{inp}}$ , and responses  $\mathbf{a} = (a_1, a_2) \in \mathbb{F}_p^2$ , the verifier first checks that  $a_1 \in [-b_1(\tau, \delta), b_1(\tau, \delta)]$  and  $a_2 \in [-b_2(\tau, \delta), b_2(\tau, \delta)]$ . If both checks pass, the verifier outputs 1 if  $a_1^2 + a_2 = a_{\mathsf{inp}} + u_C$ , and 0 otherwise.

**Theorem B.14** (Completeness). Construction B.13 satisfies perfect completeness.

*Proof.* Same as the proofs of Theorems B.2 and B.9.  $\Box$ 

**Theorem B.15** (Soundness). Construction B.13 has soundness error  $2/\tau$ .

*Proof.* Same as the proof of Theorem B.10.  $\Box$ 

**Remark B.16.** Construction B.13 has knowledge soundness  $2/\tau$ . The argument is identical to the argument at Remark B.6

<span id="page-55-0"></span>Remark B.17 (Strong Soundness of Construction B.13). Construction B.13 does *not* provide strong soundness. Notably, a malicious prover can choose the smudging factor  $z_{s+1}$  to be outside the allowable range. By design,  $z_{s+1}$  adds a linear shift to the first response  $a_1$ . Since the verifier checks whether  $a_1 \in [-b_1(\tau, \delta), b_1(\tau, \delta)]$ , there exist values of  $z_{s+1}$  near the "border" that will cause the honest verifier to reject with probability between 1 and  $2/\tau$ , which violates strong soundness.

<span id="page-55-1"></span>**Theorem B.18** (Bounded). Construction B.13 is a bounded linear PCP with bound functions  $b_1(\tau, \delta) = s\tau/2 + 2\tau\sqrt{s/2\cdot\ln(4/\delta)}/\delta$ , and  $b_2(\tau) = 2(b_1(\tau))^2$ . Moreover, for any  $\varepsilon > 0$ , Construction B.13 is bounded with respect to bound functions  $b_1'(\tau) = \tau\sqrt{s/2}\left(\sqrt{\ln(2/\varepsilon)} + 2/\delta\sqrt{\ln(4/\delta)}\right)$  and  $b_2'(\tau) = 2(b_1(\tau))^2$  with probability at least  $1 - \varepsilon$ .

*Proof.* Same as the proof of Theorem B.12, except the value of  $a_1$  can now increase by the magnitude of the smudging factor  $z_{s+1}$ , which is sampled from a bounded interval with magnitude at most  $2\tau\sqrt{s/2\cdot\ln(4/\delta)}/\delta$ . The claim follows.

**Theorem B.19** ( $\delta$ -HVZK). Construction B.13 satisfies  $\delta$ -HVZK.

*Proof.* Our analysis relies on the following standard noise smudging lemma.

<span id="page-55-2"></span>**Lemma B.20** (Noise Smudging (cf. [AJL<sup>+</sup>12])). Let  $B_1$  and  $B_2$  be positive integers. Let  $x \in [-B_1, B_1]$  be a fixed integer. Sample  $y \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} [-B_2, B_2]$ . Then, the distribution of y is  $\varepsilon$ -close to the distribution of y + x for  $\varepsilon = B_1/B_2$ .

We construct a simulator S as follows:

• On input a bound parameter  $\tau \in \mathbb{N}$  and a statement  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathcal{L}$ , sample  $(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{Q}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\tau)$ , where  $\mathsf{st} = u_C$ . Let  $a_{\mathsf{inp}} \leftarrow \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}$ .

{56}------------------------------------------------

- Sample  $\tilde{a}_1 \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} [-B, B]$  where  $B = 2\tau \sqrt{s/2 \cdot \ln(4/\delta)}/\delta$  and set  $\tilde{a}_2 \leftarrow a_{\mathsf{inp}} + u_C a_1^2$ . Let  $\tilde{\mathbf{a}} = (\tilde{a}_1, \tilde{a}_2) \in \mathbb{F}_p^2$ .
- Output  $(st, \mathbf{q}_{inp}, \mathbf{Q}, a_{inp}, \tilde{\mathbf{a}})$ .

By construction, the simulator samples  $\operatorname{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{Q}, a_{\mathsf{inp}}$  from exactly the same distribution as in the real distribution. It suffices to argue that the simulated set of responses  $\tilde{\mathbf{a}}$  is properly distributed. In the real distribution,  $\mathbf{a} \leftarrow \mathbf{Q}^{\mathsf{T}} \boldsymbol{\pi}$  where  $\boldsymbol{\pi} \leftarrow \mathcal{P}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\tau, x, w)$ . First, since  $x \in \mathcal{L}$ , in the real distribution, the verifier accepts which means that  $a_1^2 + a_2 = a_{\mathsf{inp}} + u_C$ . This means that as long as  $a_1$  and  $\tilde{a}_1$  are  $\delta$ -close, then the real and simulated distributions are  $\delta$ -close. We consider the distribution of  $a_1$  in the real distribution. In the real distribution

$$a_1 = \mathbf{q}_1^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\pi} = (\mathbf{v}')^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z}' + z_{s+1},$$

where  $\mathbf{z}' \in \mathbb{F}_p^s$  denotes the first s components of  $\mathbf{z}$ . By the same analysis as in the proof of Theorem B.12, we have that for  $B' = \tau \sqrt{s/2 \cdot \ln(4/\delta)}$ ,

$$\Pr[|(\mathbf{v}')^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{z}'| \ge B'] \le \delta/2.$$

Thus, with probability  $1 - \delta/2$ ,  $(\mathbf{v}')^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{z}' \in [-B', B']$ . Since  $B'/B = \delta/2$ , we can apply Lemma B.20 to conclude that over the randomness of  $z_{s+1}$ , the distribution of  $a_1 = (\mathbf{v}')^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{z}' + z_{s+1}$  in the real distribution is  $(\delta/2)$ -close to the distribution of  $\tilde{a}_1$  in the simulated distribution (where  $a_1$  is uniform over  $[-\delta', \delta']$ ).

## <span id="page-56-0"></span>C The Generic Group Model

In this work, we analyze the security of some of our constructions in the generic group model [Nec94, Sho97]. In the generic group model, access to the group elements is replaced by "handles." An adversary in the generic group model is also given access to a stateful oracle which implements the group operation. The generic group oracle maintains internally a mapping from handles to group elements, which it uses in order to consistently answer the oracle queries. Thus, when a scheme is shown to satisfy some security property in the generic group model, it means that no efficient adversary that only applies the group operations as a black-box can break that security property. Our presentation is adapted from similar definitions from [Zim15, KLM<sup>+</sup>18].

<span id="page-56-1"></span>**Definition C.1** (Generic Group Oracle). A generic group oracle is a stateful oracle  $\mathcal{G}$  that responds to queries GGM.Setup, GGM.Encode, GGM.Add, GGM.Test as follows:

- On a query  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda)$ , the generic group oracle samples two fresh nonces  $\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk} \overset{\mathtt{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^\lambda$  and a prime p. It outputs  $(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}, p)$ . The oracle stores the values generated, initialize an empty table  $\mathsf{T} \leftarrow \{\}$ , and set the internal state so subsequent invocations of  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}$  fail (with output  $\bot$ ).
- On a query  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(k,x)$  where  $k \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ ,  $x \in \mathbb{F}_p$ , the oracle checks that  $k = \mathsf{sk}$  (returning  $\bot$  if the check fails). The oracle then generates a fresh nonce  $\xi \stackrel{\mathsf{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , adds the entry  $\xi \mapsto x$  to the table  $\mathsf{T}$ , and replies with  $\xi$ .
- On a query  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}(k,\xi_1,\xi_2)$  where  $k,\xi_1,\xi_2 \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the oracle checks that  $k = \mathsf{pp}$ , that the handles  $\xi_1,\xi_2$  are present in its internal table T, and are mapped to values  $x_1,x_2 \in \mathbb{F}_p$ , respectively (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle samples a fresh handle  $\xi \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  and adds the entry  $\xi \mapsto (x_1 + x_2)$  to T, and replies with  $\xi$ . The addition oracle can be used to implement scalar multiplication by arbitrary  $\mathbb{F}_p$  elements via repeated doubling.
- On a query  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Test}(k,\xi)$  where  $k,x\in\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the oracle checks that  $k=\mathsf{pp}$ , that the handle  $\xi$  is present in  $\mathsf{T}$ , and that  $\xi$  maps to some value  $x\in\mathbb{F}_p$  (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle returns "zero" if  $x=0\in\mathbb{F}_p$  and "nonzero" otherwise.

{57}------------------------------------------------

Remark C.2 (Unique Encodings). Many formulations, including [Sho97], model the generic group using a random injective function  $\sigma \colon \mathbb{F}_p \to \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . In this formulation, every value in  $\mathbb{F}_p$  has a unique encoding, and there is no need for an explicit GGM. Test procedure (GGM. Test would just correspond to equality of bitstrings). Our formulation in Definition C.1 (which follows [Zim15, KLM+18]) samples a new encoding on every query and provides an explicit GGM. Test procedure for checking whether an element is an encoding of 0 (or equivalently, whether two elements are equal). We can implement a generic group model with unique encodings by using the GGM. Test procedure to test equality against the existing entries in the table T after each GGM. Encode and GGM. Add query, and returning the previously-computed handle if it is already present in the table. Otherwise, a new handle is sampled as usual. This transformation incurs a quadratic overhead in the number of queries. Thus, without loss of generality, we can assume fresh handles are output by GGM. Encode and GGM. Add, and equality-checking is handled through an explicit algorithm GGM. Test.

<span id="page-57-2"></span>Remark C.3 (Oracle Queries as Formal Polynomials [Zim15, Remark 2.11, adapted]). Although the generic group oracle is defined formally in terms of "handles" (Definition C.1), it is oftentimes more conducive to regard each oracle query as referring to a formal query polynomial. The formal variables in this formal query polynomial are specified by the expressions supplied to the GGM.Encode oracle (as determined by the details of the construction), and the adversary can construct terms that refer to new polynomials by making queries to the group operation oracle GGM.Add. Rather than operating on a "handle," each valid GGM.Test query refers to a formal query polynomial, and the result of the query is "zero" if the polynomial evaluates to zero when its variables are instantiated with the joint distribution over their values in  $\mathbb{F}_p$  as generated in the real security game.

### <span id="page-57-0"></span>C.1 Linear Targeted Malleability of ElGamal

In this section, we show that the ElGamal encryption scheme [ElG84] satisfies linear targeted malleability (Definition A.6) in the generic group model. We begin by recalling the ElGamal encryption scheme where the message is encoded in the exponent.

<span id="page-57-1"></span>Construction C.4 (ElGamal Encryption [ElG84]). Let GroupGen be a prime-order group generation algorithm. The ElGamal encryption scheme  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}} = (\mathsf{Setup}, \mathsf{Encrypt}, \mathsf{Decrypt}, \mathsf{Add}, \mathsf{ImVer})$  with message space  $\mathbb{F}_p$  is defined as follows:

- Setup(1 $^{\lambda}$ ): On input the security parameter  $\lambda$ , the setup algorithm computes ( $\mathbb{G}, p, g$ )  $\leftarrow$  GroupGen(1 $^{\lambda}$ ), samples  $x \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$ , and computes  $h \leftarrow g^x$ . It outputs the public key  $\mathsf{pk} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g, h)$  and the secret key  $\mathsf{sk} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g, x)$ . The message space for the ElGamal encryption scheme is  $\mathbb{F}_p$ .
- Encrypt(pk, a): On input the public key pk =  $(\mathbb{G}, p, g, h)$  and a message  $a \in \mathbb{F}_p$ , the encryption algorithm samples  $r \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$  and outputs the ciphertext ct =  $(g^r, h^r g^a)$ .
- Decrypt(sk, ct,  $\mathcal{M}$ ): On input a secret key sk =  $(\mathbb{G}, p, g, x)$ , a ciphertext ct =  $(\mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2)$  and a set of candidate messages  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{F}_p$ , the decryption algorithm checks whether there exists  $a \in \mathcal{M}$  such that  $g^a = \mathsf{ct}_2/\mathsf{ct}_1^x$ . If so, it outputs a; otherwise, it outputs  $\bot$ .
- Add(pk, ct<sub>1</sub>, ct<sub>2</sub>): On input the public key pk =  $(\mathbb{G}, p, g, h)$  and ciphertexts ct<sub>1</sub> =  $(g^{r_1}, h^{r_1}g^{a_1})$ , ct<sub>2</sub> =  $(g^{r_2}, h^{r_2}g^{a_2})$ , the addition algorithm outputs ct' =  $(g^{r_1}g^{r_2}, (h^{r_1}g^{a_1}) \cdot (h^{r_2}g^{a_2}))$ .
- ImVer(sk, ct,  $\mathcal{M}$ ): On input a secret key sk, a ciphertext ct and a set of candidate messages  $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{F}_p$ , the image-verification algorithm outputs 1 if Decrypt(sk, ct,  $\mathcal{M}$ )  $\neq \bot$  and 0 otherwise.

**Theorem C.5** (Correctness and Security of ElGamal [ElG84]). Construction C.4 is correct, and moreover, if the Decisional Diffie-Hellman (DDH) assumption holds with respect to GroupGen, then Construction C.4 is semantically secure.

<span id="page-57-3"></span>**Theorem C.6** (Linear Targeted Malleability of ElGamal). The ElGamal encryption scheme  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  from Construction C.4 satisfies linear targeted malleability with respect to any target message space  $\mathcal{M}$  where  $|\mathcal{M}| = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  (Definition A.6) in the generic group model.

{58}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* Take any efficient adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  and plaintext generator  $\mathcal{T}$  for the linear targeted malleability game. We construct an efficient simulator  $\mathcal{S}$  that works as follows:

- 1. At the beginning of the security game, the simulator receives the public key pk and (possibly) auxiliary input z. In the generic group model, the public key consists of pk = (pp, p, g, h) where  $pp, g, h \in \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$  are arbitrary bit strings. The simulator initializes an empty table T and adds mappings  $g \mapsto 1$  and  $h \mapsto \hat{x}$ , where  $\hat{x}$  is a formal variable (that represents the secret key of the encryption scheme, unknown to the simulator).
- 2. For each  $i \in [m]$ , the simulator samples  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  and sets  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow (\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2})$ . The simulator also adds mappings  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} \mapsto \hat{r}_i$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \mapsto \hat{r}_i \hat{x} + \hat{a}_i$ , where  $\hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m$  and  $\hat{a}_1, \ldots, \hat{a}_m$  are formal variables representing the encryption randomness and the messages.
- 3. The simulator starts running the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  on input  $\mathsf{pk} = (\mathsf{pp}, p, g, h), \, \mathsf{ct}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_m, \, \mathsf{and} \, z$ . The simulator  $\mathcal{S}$  will simulate the generic group oracle queries for  $\mathcal{A}$  as follows:
  - GGM.Setup: The simulator replies to A with  $\bot$ .
  - GGM.Encode: The simulator replies to A with  $\bot$ .
  - GGM.Add: On input a key k and handles  $\xi_1, \xi_2 \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the simulator checks that k = pp and that  $\xi_1, \xi_2$  are present in T and mapped to formal polynomials  $f_1, f_2$  over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle samples a fresh handle  $\xi \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  and adds the entry  $\xi \mapsto (f_1 + f_2)$  to T and replies with  $\xi$ .
  - GGM.Test: On input a key k and a handle  $\xi \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the simulator checks that k = pp, that  $\xi$  is present in T, and mapped to a formal polynomial f over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle outputs "zero" if  $f \equiv 0$  is the identically-zero polynomial over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  and "nonzero" otherwise.
- 4. At the end of the experiment, the adversary outputs a collection of ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}_1', \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_k'$ , where each  $\mathsf{ct}_i' = (\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}', \mathsf{ct}_{i,2}')$ . The simulator first checks that each  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}' \mapsto f_{i,1}$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2}' \mapsto f_{i,2}$  in T. If this is not the case for any  $i \in [k]$ , the simulator outputs  $\bot$ .
- 5. By construction of the simulator, every  $f_{i,1}$  and  $f_{i,2}$  can be expressed as a linear function in the variables  $\hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m, \hat{a}_1, \ldots, \hat{a}_m, \hat{x}$ . The simulator now checks that  $f_{i,2} \hat{x} f_{i,1} \equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [m]} \alpha_{i,j} \hat{a}_j\right) + \beta_i$ , for some choice of scalars  $\alpha_{i,1}, \ldots, \alpha_{i,m}, \beta_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$ . If this relation does not hold for any  $i \in [k]$ , then the simulator outputs  $\bot$ . Otherwise, the simulator sets the  $i^{\text{th}}$  row of  $\mathbf{\Pi} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{k \times m}$  to  $[\alpha_{i,1}, \ldots, \alpha_{i,m}]$  and the  $i^{\text{th}}$  component of  $\mathbf{b} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$  to  $\beta_i$ .
- 6. Output the matrix  $\mathbf{\Pi} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{k \times m}$  and the vector  $\mathbf{b} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$ .

We now show that the real distribution and the simulated distributions are computationally indistinguishable when GroupGen is modeled as a generic group  $\mathcal{G}$ . To do so, we use a hybrid argument:

- Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the real distribution in Definition A.6. Namely, the experiment proceeds as follows:
  - 1. The challenger samples  $(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}', p) \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}), \ g \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, 1), \ x \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p, \ \text{and} \ h \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}', x).$  It then sets  $\mathsf{pk} = (\mathsf{pp}, p, g, h)$  and  $\mathsf{sk} = (\mathsf{pp}, p, g, \mathsf{sk}', x).$
  - 2. The challenger computes  $(s, a_1, \ldots, a_m) \leftarrow \mathcal{T}(\mathsf{pk})$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, a_i)$  for each  $i \in [m]$ . Namely, for each  $i \in [m]$ , the challenger samples  $r_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$ , and computes  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}$  to be an encoding of  $r_i$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2}$  to be an encoding of  $r_i \times a_i$  (using the public components  $\mathsf{pp}, g, h$  and queries to  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}$ ). It sets  $\mathsf{ct}_i = (\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2})$ . In particular,  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} \mapsto r_i$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \mapsto r_i \times a_i$  in the internal table T maintained by the generic group oracle.

{59}------------------------------------------------

- 3. The challenger sends the public key pk, the ciphertexts  $ct_1, \ldots, ct_m$ , and the auxiliary information z to the adversary A. The adversary is also given access to the generic group oracles GGM.Setup, GGM.Encode, GGM.Add, GGM.Test.
- 4. At some point, the adversary outputs  $\mathsf{ct}_1', \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_k'$ .
- 5. If  $\mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}_i', \mathcal{M}) \neq 1$  for any  $i \in [k]$ , the challenger outputs  $\bot$ . In the case of the ElGamal scheme, this means the challenger outputs  $\bot$  unless  $\mathsf{ct}_i' = (\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}', \mathsf{ct}_{i,2}')$  where  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}' \mapsto r_i'$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2}' \mapsto r_i'x + a_i'$  in the internal table T maintained by the generic group oracle for some  $r_i' \in \mathbb{F}_p$  and  $a_i' \in \mathcal{X}$ . If  $\mathsf{ct}_i'$  satisfy this requirement for all  $i \in [k]$ , then the output of the experiment is  $(\mathsf{pk}, \{a_i\}_{i \in [m]}, s, \{a_i'\}_{i \in [k]})$ .
- $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ : Same as  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  except the challenger computes the ciphertext components  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2}$  by directly encoding the value of  $r_i$  and  $r_i x + a_i$ , respectively, using the secret key  $\mathsf{sk}'$ . In other words, the challenger computes  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}', r_i)$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}', r_i x + a_i)$ .
- $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ : Same as  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ , except the challenger implements the generic group oracles according to the specification of  $\mathcal{S}$ . In particular, the challenger proceeds as follows in this experiment:
  - 1. The challenger runs  $(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}', p) \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ . Then, it samples  $x \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$ , initializes an empty table T, samples two strings  $g, h \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ , and adds the mappings  $g \mapsto 1$  and  $h \mapsto \hat{x}$  to T. Here  $\hat{x}$  is a formal variable representing the secret key. The challenger sets  $\mathsf{pk} = (\mathsf{pp}, p, g, h)$ .
  - 2. The challenger computes  $(s, a_1, \ldots, a_m) \leftarrow \mathcal{T}(\mathsf{pk})$ . For each  $i \in [m]$ , it samples  $r_i \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . It adds mappings  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} \mapsto \hat{r}_i$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \mapsto \hat{r}_i \hat{x} + \hat{a}_i$ , where  $\hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m$  and  $\hat{a}_1, \ldots, \hat{a}_m$  are formal variables for the encryption randomness and the message, respectively.
  - 3. The challenger sends the public key pk, the ciphertexts  $ct_1, \ldots, ct_m$ , and the auxiliary information z to A. It then responds to the generic group oracle queries using the same procedure as the simulator S (with respect to its own table T).
  - 4. At the end of the experiment, the adversary outputs ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}_1', \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_k'$ .
  - 5. The challenger now scans through the values in T and instantiates each of the formal variables  $\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m, \hat{a}_1, \ldots, \hat{a}_m$  with their real values  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m, a_1, \ldots, a_m$ , respectively. The output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  is then computed using the same procedure as in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ .
- Hyb<sub>3</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>2</sub> except after the adversary outputs  $\mathsf{ct}_1', \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_k'$ , instantiating the formal variables  $\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m, \hat{a}_1, \ldots, \hat{a}_m$  with their values  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m, a_1, \ldots, a_m$ , and verifying ImVer(sk,  $\mathsf{ct}_i', \mathcal{M}$ ) = 1 for all  $i \in [k]$ , the challenger computes the output using the following procedure:
  - 1. The challenger parses  $\mathsf{ct}_i'$  as  $(\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}', \mathsf{ct}_{i,2}')$ , and checks that  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}' \mapsto f_{i,1}$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \mapsto f_{i,2}$  in T. It then checks that  $f_{i,2} \hat{x}f_{i,1} \equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [m]} \alpha_{i,j}\hat{a}_j\right) + \beta_i$ , for some choice of scalars  $\alpha_{i,1}, \ldots, \alpha_{i,m}, \beta_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$ . If this relation does not hold for any  $i \in [k]$ , then the challenger outputs  $\bot$ .
  - 2. Let  $\mathbf{\Pi} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{k \times m}$  be the matrix whose  $i^{\text{th}}$  row is  $[\alpha_{i,1}, \dots, \alpha_{i,m}]$  and let  $\mathbf{b} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$  be the vector whose  $i^{\text{th}}$  component is  $\beta_i$ . The challenger computes  $(a'_1, \dots, a'_k)^{\mathsf{T}} \leftarrow \mathbf{\Pi} \cdot (a_1, \dots, a_m) + \mathbf{b}$ .
  - 3. Output the tuple  $(pk, \{a_i\}_{i \in [m]}, s, \{a'_i\}_{i \in [k]})$ .

This is the ideal distribution with simulator S in Definition A.6.

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output of an execution of the game described by  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We now show that each adjacent pair of hybrids are statistically indistinguishable to any adversary that makes polynomially-many queries to the generic group oracle.

<span id="page-59-0"></span>**Lemma C.7.** For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  making  $poly(\lambda)$  queries to  $\mathcal{G}$ ,  $Hyb_0(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} Hyb_1(\mathcal{A})$ .

{60}------------------------------------------------

Proof. The only difference between  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is that the challenger uses  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}$  to construct the ciphertext components  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2}$  rather than  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}$ . Let  $t = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  be the number of queries to  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}$  the challenger makes to  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}$  to compute  $\mathsf{ct}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{ct}_m$ . The only difference between  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is that computing  $\mathsf{ct}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{ct}_m$  introduces t entries into the generic group table  $\mathsf{T}$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  while computing  $\mathsf{ct}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{ct}_m$  introduces t entries into the  $\mathsf{T}$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ . However, these additional entries in  $\mathsf{T}$  are information-theoretically hidden from the view of t, and the view of t is identically distributed in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  unless t makes a query to t0 on an encoding present in t1 in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  but not in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ . There are at most t2 such encodings. Since the encodings are uniform over t3, and the adversary makes t4 makes t5 poly(t6) queries, the probability that the adversary makes a query on one of these encodings is at most t5 negl(t6). Thus, the view of t6 in t7 in t8 is statistically indistinguishable and the claim holds.

## <span id="page-60-1"></span>**Lemma C.8.** For all adversaries $\mathcal{A}$ making $poly(\lambda)$ queries to $\mathcal{G}$ , $Hyb_1(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} Hyb_2(\mathcal{A})$ .

Proof. We show that the view of the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  is statistically indistinguishable in the two experiments. By construction, the public key  $\mathsf{pk} = (\mathsf{pp}, p, g, h)$ , the ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_m$ , and the auxiliary data z are identically distributed in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ . Namely, in both experiments,  $\mathsf{pp}, g, h$  are uniform random strings over  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , and each ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct}_i$  consists of two uniformly random strings over  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . It suffices to argue that each of the adversary's queries to the generic group oracle are statistically indistinguishable. We use a hybrid argument over the number of queries the adversary makes, where in the  $i^{\text{th}}$  hybrid  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ , the first i queries are answered according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  while the remaining queries are answered according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ . We show that for all i, the outputs of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$  are statistically indistinguishable. It suffices to consider the  $i^{\text{th}}$  query:

- GGM.Setup: In both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ , the setup oracle outputs  $\bot$ .
- GGM.Encode: In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$ , the GGM.Encode oracle outputs  $\bot$  unless the adversary queries the oracle on  $k = \mathsf{sk}'$ . Since the view of  $\mathcal{A}$  on the first i-1 queries in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  is independent of  $\mathsf{sk}'$  and  $\mathsf{sk}'$  is uniform over  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  is  $\bot$  with probability  $1-2^{-\lambda}$ . In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ , the output is  $\bot$  with probability 1, so the output distribution on the  $i^{\mathsf{th}}$  query is statistically indistinguishable in these two experiments.
- GGM.Add: The addition oracle has identical behavior in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ . Namely, if the adversary provides  $k = \mathsf{pp}$  and two valid handles  $\xi_1, \xi_2$ , then it receives a uniformly random string in  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  as its output, and if it provides an invalid input, it receives  $\bot$ .
- GGM.Test: Suppose an adversary makes a query to GGM.Test on k = pp and  $\xi \in T$ . On all other queries, the oracle outputs  $\bot$  in both experiments. This means that  $\xi$  must have been added to T as a result of a GGM.Encode query or as a result of a GGM.Add query. By construction of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i+1}$ , this means that

<span id="page-60-0"></span>
$$\xi \mapsto \left[\underbrace{\alpha + \beta \hat{x} + \sum_{i \in [m]} (\gamma_i \hat{r}_i + \delta_i (\hat{r}_i \hat{x} + \hat{a}_i))}_{f(\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m, \hat{a}_1, \dots, \hat{a}_m)}\right], \tag{C.1}$$

for some choice of scalars  $\alpha, \beta, \gamma_1, \ldots, \gamma_m, \delta_1, \ldots, \delta_m \in \mathbb{F}_p$ . By construction, the output of GGM. Test in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  is 1 if and only if  $f(x, r_1, \ldots, r_m, a_1, \ldots, a_m) = 0$  where  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m, a_1, \ldots, a_m \in \mathbb{F}_p$  are the scalars sampled by the challenger in the experiment. In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ , the output of GGM. Test is 1 only if  $f \equiv 0$  is the identically-zero polynomial (in the formal variables  $\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m, \hat{a}_1, \ldots, \hat{a}_m$ ). Thus, these two experiments only differ if  $f \not\equiv 0$ , but  $f(x, r_1, \ldots, r_m, a_1, \ldots, a_m) \not\equiv 0$ . We show that this happens with negligible probability. First, in both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ , the first i-1 queries are handled according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ . By construction, this means that everything in the experiment prior to the  $i^{\text{th}}$  query can be simulated without knowledge of the value of  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m$ , and these values can in fact be sampled after the adversary has submitted its  $i^{\text{th}}$  query (i.e., after the adversary has

{61}------------------------------------------------

committed to the polynomial f). First, define the polynomial g in the formal variables  $\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m$  to be the polynomial f where each of the variables  $\hat{a}_i$  are instantiated with their actual value  $a_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$ :

$$g(\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m) = f(\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m, a_1, \dots, a_m)$$

$$= \left(\alpha + \sum_{i \in [m]} \delta_i a_i\right) + \beta \hat{x} + \sum_{i \in [m]} (\gamma_i \hat{r}_i + \delta_i \hat{r}_i \hat{x})$$

It is easy to see that if  $f \not\equiv 0$ , then  $g \not\equiv 0$  (since if  $g \equiv 0$ , then it must be the case that  $\beta, \gamma_i, \delta_i = 0$  for all  $i \in [m]$ , which correspondingly means that  $\alpha = 0$ , in which case  $f \equiv 0$ ). Since g is a polynomial of total degree 2 in the variables  $\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m$ , we conclude by the Schwartz-Zippel lemma that

$$\Pr[g(x, r_1, \dots, r_m) = 0 \mid x, r_1, \dots, r_m \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p] \le 2/p = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Thus, with overwhelming probability over the choice of  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m$ , if  $f \not\equiv 0$ , then

$$f(x, r_1, \dots, r_m, a_1, \dots, a_m) = g(x, r_1, \dots, r_m) \neq 0,$$

in which case the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  is 0, which coincides with the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ .

Since the adversary makes a polynomial number of queries to the generic group oracle, and the response to each query is statistically indistinguishable, we conclude via a hybrid argument that the adversary's output in the two distributions are statistically indistinguishable. Since the output of the experiment is computed using the same procedure in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ , we conclude that the outputs of the two distributions are also statistically indistinguishable.

## <span id="page-61-0"></span>**Lemma C.9.** For all adversaries $\mathcal{A}$ , $\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_3(\mathcal{A})$ .

Proof. By construction, the view of  $\mathcal{A}$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  is identically distributed. Thus, the ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}'_i, \ldots, \mathsf{ct}'_k$  output by  $\mathcal{A}$  in the two experiments are identically distributed. It suffices to argue that the output in the two experiments are statistically indistinguishable. We write  $\mathsf{ct}'_i = (\mathsf{ct}'_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}'_{i,2})$  where each  $\mathsf{ct}'_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}'_{i,2} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . In addition, we note that in both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$ , all of the interactions with  $\mathcal{A}$  can be implemented without knowledge of the values of  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m$ . Thus, we can defer the sampling of  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$  until after the adversary has output  $\mathsf{ct}'_1, \ldots, \mathsf{ct}'_k$ . We consider several possibilities:

- Suppose that there exists  $i \in [k]$  where  $\mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}_i', \mathcal{M}) = 0$ . Then, both experiments output  $\bot$ .
- Suppose that there exists  $i \in [k]$  where  $\mathsf{ct}'_{i,1} \notin \mathsf{T}$  or  $\mathsf{ct}'_{i,2} \notin \mathsf{T}$ . Then,  $\mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}'_i, \mathcal{M}) = 0$ , and both experiments output  $\bot$ .
- Suppose  $\operatorname{ct}'_{i,1} \mapsto f_{i,1}$  and  $\operatorname{ct}'_{i,2} \mapsto f_{i,2}$  in T, but  $f_{i,2} \hat{x}f_{i,1} \not\equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [m]} \alpha_{i,j}\hat{a}_j\right) + \beta_i$  for some  $i \in [k]$ . We argue that with overwhelming probability,  $\operatorname{ImVer}(\operatorname{sk}, \operatorname{ct}'_i, \mathcal{M}) = 0$  and both experiments output  $\bot$ . Take any  $a \in \mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathbb{F}_p$  and define the polynomial  $g_a$  in the formal variables  $\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \ldots, \hat{r}_m$ :

$$g_a(\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m) = f_{i,2}(\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m, a_1, \dots, a_m) - \hat{x} \cdot f_{i,1}(\hat{x}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m, a_1, \dots, a_m) - a.$$

By the structure of  $f_{i,1}$  and  $f_{i,2}$  (Eq. (C.1)) and the assumption that  $f_{i,2} - \hat{x} f_{i,1} \not\equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [m]} \alpha_{i,j} \hat{a}_j\right) + \beta_i$ , we have that  $g_a \not\equiv 0$ . Since  $g_a$  is a polynomial of total degree 3, we can appeal to the Schwartz-Zippel lemma and conclude that for any  $a \in \mathcal{M}$ ,

$$\Pr[g_a(x, r_1, \dots, r_m) = 0 \mid x, r_1, \dots, r_m \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p] = 3/p.$$

By a union bound,

$$\Pr[\exists a \in \mathcal{M} : g_a(x, r_1, \dots, r_m) = 0 \mid x, r_1, \dots, r_m \stackrel{\text{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p] = 3|\mathcal{M}|/p = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

{62}------------------------------------------------

since  $|\mathcal{M}| = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ . Thus, with overwhelming probability over the choice of  $x, r_1, \ldots, r_m$ , we have that  $\mathsf{Decrypt}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}_i', \mathcal{M}) = \bot$ . Correspondingly, with overwhelming probability,  $\mathsf{ImVer}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}_i', \mathcal{M}) = 0$  and both experiments output  $\bot$  in this case.

• Suppose  $\operatorname{ct}'_{i,1} \mapsto f_{i,1}$  and  $\operatorname{ct}'_{i,2} \mapsto f_{i,2}$  in T where  $f_{i,2} - \hat{x}f_{i,1} \equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [m]} \alpha_{i,j}\hat{a}_j\right) + \beta_i$ , and moreover, that  $\operatorname{ImVer}(\operatorname{sk},\operatorname{ct}'_i,\mathcal{M}) = 1$  for all  $i \in [k]$ . Since  $\operatorname{ImVer}(\operatorname{sk},\operatorname{ct}',\mathcal{M}) = 1$ , there exists  $a'_i \in \mathcal{M}$  such that

$$f_{i,2}(x,r_1,\ldots,r_m,a_1,\ldots,a_m)-xf_1(x,r_1,\ldots,r_m,a_1,\ldots,a_m)=a_i'.$$

Since  $f_{i,2} - \hat{x}f_{i,1} \equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [m]} \alpha_{i,j}\hat{a}_j\right) + \beta_i$ , this means that

$$f_{i,2}(x, r_1, \dots, r_m, a_1, \dots, a_m) - x f_1(x, r_1, \dots, r_m, a_1, \dots, a_m) = \sum_{j \in [m]} \alpha_{i,j} a_j + \beta_i = a'_i.$$

Since this holds for all  $i \in [k]$ , this means that  $(a'_1, \ldots, a'_k)^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{\Pi} \cdot (a_1, \ldots, a_m)^{\mathsf{T}} + \mathbf{b}$ , where  $\mathbf{\Pi} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{k \times m}$  and  $\mathbf{b} \in \mathbb{F}_p^k$  are precisely the quantities the challenger constructs in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$ . Thus, in this case, the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  are identically distributed.

The claim now follows by combining Lemmas C.7 to C.9.

#### <span id="page-62-1"></span>C.2 Adaptive Soundness of Construction A.8 in the Generic Group Model

In this section, we show that Construction A.8 satisfies adaptive soundness when the underlying encryption scheme is instantiated with ElGamal encryption over a generic group (Construction C.4).

<span id="page-62-0"></span>**Theorem C.10.** Let  $C = \{C_\ell\}_{\ell \in \mathbb{N}}$  be a family of arithmetic circuits. Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  be the ElGamal encryption (Construction C.4) scheme with plaintext space  $\mathbb{F}_p$ , and let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  be a 1-query linear PCP over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  for the relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$  with bound function  $B = B(\lambda)$ , query length  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$  and soundness error  $\varepsilon = \varepsilon(\lambda)$ . Then Construction A.8 is a preprocessing SNARG for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}}$  with perfect completeness and adaptive soundness error  $\varepsilon + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  when the group  $\mathbb{G}$  in the ElGamal encryption scheme is modeled as a generic group.

*Proof.* Completeness follows exactly as in Theorem A.9, so it suffices to argue adaptive soundness. We use a hybrid argument similar to that used in the proof of Theorem C.6. As in the proof of Theorem C.6, we model GroupGen as a generic group  $\mathcal{G}$ .

- Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the adaptive soundness experiment:
  - 1. The challenger begins by sampling  $(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}, p) \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda), g \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, 1), z \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p,$  and  $h \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, z)$ . It sets  $\mathsf{pk} = (\mathsf{pp}, p, g, h)$ . Next, the challenger samples  $(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{q}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  where  $\mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}} \in \mathbb{F}_p^n$  and  $\mathbf{q} = (q_1, \ldots, q_\ell) \in \mathbb{F}_p^\ell$ . Then, the setup algorithm computes the ciphertexts  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Encrypt}(\mathsf{pk}, q_i)$  for each  $i \in [\ell]$ . For each  $i \in [\ell]$ , the challenger samples  $r_i \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$  and computes  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}$  to be an encoding of  $r_i$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2}$  to be an encoding of  $r_iz + q_i$  (using the public components  $\mathsf{pp}, g, h$  and queries to  $\mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Add}$ ). It sets  $\mathsf{ct}_i = (\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2})$ . In particular,  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} \mapsto r_i$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \mapsto r_iz + q_i$  in the internal table T maintained by the generic group oracle.
  - 2. The challenger gives  $\operatorname{crs} = (\operatorname{pk}, \{\operatorname{ct}_i\}_{i \in [\ell]})$  to the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . In addition, the adversary is given access to the generic group oracles GGM.Setup, GGM.Encode, GGM.Add, GGM.Test.
  - 3. At some point, the adversary outputs a statement  $\mathbf{x}$  and a proof  $\pi$ . If  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathcal{C}}$ , then the challenger outputs 0. Otherwise, the challenger parses  $\pi = (\mathsf{ct}_1', \mathsf{ct}_2')$  and checks that  $\mathsf{ct}_1' \mapsto r$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_2' \mapsto rz + a$  for some  $r \in \mathbb{F}_p$  and  $a \in [-B, B]$  in the internal table T maintained by the generic group oracle. If not, the challenger outputs 0. Otherwise, the challenger outputs  $\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}, a)$ .
- $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ : Same as  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  except the challenger computes the ciphertext components  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2}$  by directly encoding the value of  $r_i$  and  $r_iz + q_i$  using the secret key sk. Specifically, the challenger computes  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, r_i)$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Encode}(\mathsf{sk}, r_iz + q_i)$ .

{63}------------------------------------------------

- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub> except the challenger implements the generic group oracle queries with the following modified procedure:
  - 1. The challenger starts by running  $(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}, p) \leftarrow \mathsf{GGM}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$  and samples  $z \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$ . It then initializes an empty table T, samples two strings  $g, h \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$  and adds the mappings  $g \mapsto 1$  and  $h \mapsto \hat{z}$  to T. Here,  $\hat{z}$  is a formal variable representing the secret key. The challenger sets  $\mathsf{pk} = (\mathsf{pp}, p, g, h)$ .
  - 2. Next, for each  $i \in [\ell]$ , the challenger samples  $r_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$ , and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . It adds mappings  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} \mapsto \hat{r}_i$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_{i,2} \mapsto \hat{r}_i \hat{z} + \hat{q}_i$  where  $\hat{r}_i, \ldots, \hat{r}_\ell$  and  $\hat{q}_1, \ldots, \hat{q}_\ell$  are formal variables for the encryption randomness and the query component, respectively. It still sets  $\mathsf{ct}_i = (\mathsf{ct}_{i,1}, \mathsf{ct}_{i,2})$  as before.
  - 3. The challenger gives  $\operatorname{crs} = (\operatorname{pk}, \{\operatorname{ct}_i\}_{i \in [\ell]})$  to the adversary. It then responds to the adversary's generic group queries as follows:
    - GGM.Setup: The challenger replies to  $\mathcal{A}$  with  $\perp$ .
    - GGM.Encode: The challenger replies to  $\mathcal{A}$  with  $\perp$ .
    - GGM.Add: On input a key k and handles  $\xi_1, \xi_2 \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the simulator checks that k = pp and that  $\xi_1, \xi_2$  are present in T and mapped to formal polynomials  $f_1, f_2$  over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle samples a fresh handle  $\xi \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  and adds the entry  $\xi \mapsto (f_1 + f_2)$  to T and replies with  $\xi$ .
    - GGM.Test: On input a key k and a handle  $\xi \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the simulator checks that k = pp, that  $\xi$  is present in T, and mapped to a formal polynomial f over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  (returning  $\perp$  otherwise). If the checks pass, the oracle outputs "zero" if  $f \equiv 0$  is the identically-zero polynomial over  $\mathbb{F}_p$  and "nonzero" otherwise.
  - 4. At the end of the experiment, the adversary outputs a statement  $\mathbf{x}$  and a proof  $\pi$ . If  $\mathbf{x} \notin \mathcal{L}_{\mathcal{C}}$ , then the adversary outputs 0. Otherwise, the challenger scans through the values in T and instantiates each of the formal variables  $\hat{z}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_{\ell}, \hat{q}_1, \dots, \hat{q}_{\ell}$  with their real values  $z, r_1, \dots, r_{\ell}, q_1, \dots, q_{\ell}$ , respectively. The output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  is computed using the same procedure as in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ .

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output of experiment  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We now show that the output distribution of any two adjacent hybrid experiments is statistically indistinguishable. Finally, we show that the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A})$  is 0 with probability negligibly close to  $\varepsilon$ .

<span id="page-63-0"></span>**Lemma C.11.** For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  making  $poly(\lambda)$  queries to  $\mathcal{G}$ ,  $Hyb_0(\mathcal{A}) \stackrel{s}{\approx} Hyb_1(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* Follows by a same analysis as the proof of Lemma C.7.

**Lemma C.12.** For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  making  $poly(\lambda)$  queries to  $\mathcal{G}$ ,  $Hyb_1(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} Hyb_2(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* Follows by a similar analysis as the proof of Lemma C.8.

<span id="page-63-1"></span>**Lemma C.13.** If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  is sound, then  $\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A}) = 1] \leq \varepsilon(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

*Proof.* This follows by a similar analysis as the proof of Lemma C.9. First, we note that in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ , the adversary's view is entirely independent of the linear PCP query  $\mathbf{q} \in \mathbb{F}_p^{\ell}$ . This means that the challenger can sample  $(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_\mathsf{inp}, \mathbf{q})$  after the adversary outputs the statement  $\mathbf{x}$  and proof  $\pi$ . Similarly, the adversary's view is also independent of the value of the secret key z as well as the encryption randomness  $r_1, \ldots, r_\ell$ , so the challenger can also sample these values after the adversary has chosen its output. Write  $\pi = (\mathsf{ct}_1', \mathsf{ct}_2')$ . We consider several possibilities:

- If the adversary outputs a statement  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathcal{L}$ , then the output of the experiment is 0.
- If either  $\mathsf{ct}_1' \notin \mathsf{T}$  or  $\mathsf{ct}_2' \notin \mathsf{T}$ , then the experiment outputs 0.

{64}------------------------------------------------

• Suppose  $\operatorname{ct}_1' \mapsto f_1$  and  $\operatorname{ct}_2' \mapsto f_2$  in T, but  $f_2 - \hat{z}f_1 \not\equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [\ell]} \alpha_j \hat{q}_j\right) + \beta$ , for some set of scalars  $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_\ell, \beta \in \mathbb{F}_p$ . By construction of  $\operatorname{\mathsf{Hyb}}_2$ , every encoding  $\xi$  in T is a formal polynomial of the following form:

$$\xi \mapsto \left[\underbrace{\bar{\alpha} + \bar{\beta}\hat{z} + \sum_{j \in [\ell]} (\bar{\gamma}_j \hat{r}_j + \bar{\delta}_j (\hat{r}_j \hat{x} + \hat{q}_j))}_{f(\hat{z}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_\ell, \hat{q}_1, \dots, \hat{q}_\ell)}\right],$$

for some choice of scalars  $\bar{\alpha}, \bar{\beta}, \bar{\gamma}_1, \dots, \bar{\gamma}_\ell, \bar{\delta}_1, \dots, \bar{\delta}_\ell \in \mathbb{F}_p$ . We argue that with overwhelming probability over the choice of  $z, r_1, \dots, r_\ell$ , the output of the experiment is 0 in this case. Take any  $a \in [-B, B]$ , and define the polynomial  $g_a$  in the formal variables  $\hat{z}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m$ :

$$g_a(\hat{z}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_m) = f_2(\hat{z}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_\ell, q_1, \dots, q_m) - \hat{z} \cdot f_1(\hat{z}, \hat{r}_1, \dots, \hat{r}_\ell, q_1, \dots, q_m) - a.$$

Since  $f_2 - \hat{z}f_1 \not\equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [\ell]} \alpha_j \hat{q}_j\right) + \beta$ , we have that  $g_a \not\equiv 0$ . Since  $g_a$  is a polynomial of total degree 3, we can appeal to the Schwartz-Zippel lemma and conclude that for any  $a \in [-B, B]$ ,

$$\Pr[g_a(z, r_1, \dots, r_\ell) = 0 \mid z, r_1, \dots, r_\ell \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p] = 3/p.$$

By a union bound,

$$\Pr[\exists a \in [-B, B] : g_a(z, r_1, \dots, r_\ell) = 0 \mid z, r_1, \dots, r_\ell \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p] = 6B/p = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

since  $B = \text{poly}(\lambda)$ . Thus, with overwhelming probability over the choice of  $z, r_1, \ldots, r_\ell$ , the output of the experiment is 0 in this case.

• Suppose that  $\mathbf{x} \notin \mathcal{L}$  and  $\operatorname{ct}_1' \mapsto f_1$  and  $\operatorname{ct}_2' \mapsto f_2$  in T where  $f_2 - \hat{z}f_1 \equiv \left(\sum_{j \in [\ell]} \alpha_j \hat{q}_j\right) + \beta$ . Consider the value of  $f_2 - \hat{z}f_1$  instantiated with the values of  $z, r_1, \ldots, r_\ell, q_1, \ldots, q_\ell$ :

$$f_2(z, r_1, \dots, r_\ell, q_1, \dots, q_\ell) - z \cdot f_1(z, r_1, \dots, r_\ell, q_1, \dots, q_\ell) = \sum_{j \in [\ell]} \alpha_j q_j + \beta = \mathbf{q}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\alpha} + \beta,$$

where  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}_p^{\ell}$  is the vector of  $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_{\ell}$ . As noted above, the linear PCP query  $(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{q})$  is sampled after the adversary has chosen  $\mathsf{ct}_1'$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_2'$  (and correspondingly, after it has committed to the vector  $\alpha$  and the offset  $\beta$ ). Since  $\mathbf{x} \notin \mathcal{L}$ , we can appeal to soundness of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{LPCP}}$  and conclude

$$\Pr[\mathcal{V}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{q}^\mathsf{T} \boldsymbol{\alpha} + \boldsymbol{\beta}) = 1 \mid (\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{q}_{\mathsf{inp}}, \mathbf{q}) \leftarrow \mathcal{Q}_{\mathsf{LPCP}}] \leq \varepsilon.$$

Thus, in this case, the output of the experiment is 1 with probability at most  $\varepsilon$ .

Thus, in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ , the output of the experiment is 1 with probability at most  $\varepsilon + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

The claim now follows by combining Lemmas C.11 to C.13.

# <span id="page-64-0"></span>D Proof of Lemma 4.2 (Hardness of GapMWSP)

Lemma 4.2 follows by a direct adaptation of [HKLT19, Theorem 2.1], generalized to arbitrary finite fields. We give a reduction from the NP-hard problem GapLabelCover to GapMWSP. We begin by reviewing the definition and the NP-hardness of the LabelCover problem:

**Definition D.1** (Gap Label Covering Problem (GapLabelCover) [Raz95, Hås01, HKLT19]). A LabelCover instance consists of a regular bipartite multi-graph G = (L, R, E) and two finite sets  $\Sigma_L, \Sigma_R$  where  $|\Sigma_L| \geq |\Sigma_R|$ . Every vertex in L should be assigned a label from  $\Sigma_L$  and every vertex in R should be assigned a

{65}------------------------------------------------

label in  $\Sigma_R$ . For each edge  $e \in E$  there is a projection  $\pi_e \colon \Sigma_L \to \Sigma_R$ . Given a labeling to the vertices of the graph (i.e. functions  $\phi_L \colon L \to \Sigma_L$  and  $\phi_R \colon R \to \Sigma_R$ ), an edge  $e = (a, b) \in E$  is said to be "satisfied" if  $\pi_e(\phi_L(a)) = \phi_R(b)$ . For  $1 \ge c > s > 0$ , GapLabelCover<sub>c,s</sub> is the promise problem of distinguishing whether the LabelCover instance is at least c-satisfiable (i.e., a c-fraction of the edges can be satisfied by some labeling) or at most s-satisfiable (i.e., every labeling can only satisfy at most an s-fraction of the edges).

<span id="page-65-1"></span>Theorem D.2 (NP-Hardness of GapLabelCover [MR10, DS14]). For any constant c > 0 and  $\delta = 1/\log^c n$ , GapLabelCover<sub>1,\delta</sub> is NP-hard when the LabelCover instance satisfies  $|\Sigma_L|, |\Sigma_R| \le |L| + |R|$ .

To show NP-hardness of the GapMWSP problem, we start by showing that without loss of generality, we can always assume that |L| > |R| in GapLabelCover<sub>1. $\delta$ </sub>.

<span id="page-65-0"></span>**Lemma D.3.** Let  $\mathcal{I}$  be a LabelCover instance over a regular bipartite multi-graph G=(L,R,E). Then, there is an efficient algorithm that constructs a new LabelCover instance  $\mathcal{I}'$  over a bipartite multi-graph G'=(L',R,E') such that |L'|=2|L|, every node in L' has equal degree, and for any  $0 \leq \delta \leq 1$ , instance  $\mathcal{I}$  is  $\delta$ -satisfiable if and only if  $\mathcal{I}'$  is  $\delta$ -satisfiable.

*Proof.* Let  $\mathcal{I} = (G = (L, R, E), \Sigma_L, \Sigma_R, \{\pi_e\}_{e \in E})$  be a LabelCover instance. Write  $L = \{u_1, \ldots, u_k\}$  and  $R = \{v_1, \ldots, v_\ell\}$ . We construct a LabelCover instance  $\mathcal{I}' = (G' = (L', R, E'), \Sigma_L, \Sigma_R, \{\pi'_e\}_{e \in E'})$  as follows:

- Let  $L_1 = L$  and  $L_2 = \{u'_1, \ldots, u'_k\}$  and set  $L' = L_1 \cup L_2$ .
- Let  $E_1 = E$  and  $E_2 = \{(u_i', v_j) : (u_i, v_j) \in E\}$  and set  $E' = E_1 \cup E_2$ ;
- For  $(u_i, v_j) \in E$ , set  $\pi'_{u_i, v_j}, \pi'_{u'_i, v_j} \leftarrow \pi_{u_i, v_j}$ .

By construction, |L'| = 2|L| and G' is bipartite. Since G is regular, every node in L has the same degree; the same extends to L' by construction. It suffices to show that  $\mathcal{I}$  is  $\delta$ -satisfiable if and only if  $\mathcal{I}'$  is  $\delta$ -satisfiable:

- Suppose  $\mathcal{I}$  is  $\delta$ -satisfiable. Then, there exists a labeling function  $(\phi_L, \phi_R)$  that satisfies a  $\delta$ -fraction of the edges  $e \in E$ . We define  $\phi_{L'}: L' \to \Sigma_L$  to be the mapping  $u_i, u'_i \mapsto \phi_L(u_i)$ . By construction, this satisfies a  $\delta$  fraction of the edges in  $E_1$  and  $E_2$ .
- Suppose  $\mathcal{I}'$  is  $\delta$ -satisfiable. Then, there exists a labeling function  $(\phi_{L'}, \phi_R)$  that satisfies a  $\delta$ -fraction of the edges in  $e \in E'$ . Let  $\phi_1 : L_1 \to \Sigma_L$  denote the action of  $\phi_{L'}$  restricted to  $L_1$  and let  $\phi_2 : L_2 \to \Sigma_R$  denote the action of  $\phi_{L'}$  restricted to  $L_2$ . Since  $E' = E_1 \cup E_2$  and  $|E_1| = |E_2|$ , by construction of  $E_1, E_2$ , it must be the case that either  $\phi_1$  satisfies a  $\delta$ -fraction of the edges in  $E_1$  or  $\phi_2$  satisfies a  $\delta$ -fraction of the edges in  $E_2$ . If  $\phi_1$  satisfies a  $\delta$ -fraction of the edges in  $E_1 = E$ , then  $\mathcal{I}$  is  $\delta$ -satisfiable. If  $\phi_2$  satisfies a  $\delta$ -fraction of the edges in  $E_2$ , then by construction of  $L_2$  and  $L_2$ , the labeling function  $\phi_L(u_i) := \phi_2(u_i')$  will satisfy a  $\delta$ -fraction of the constraints in  $E_1 = E$ .

Take any constant c > 0 and let  $\beta = \log^c n$ . Take any finite field  $\mathbb{F}$  where  $\log |\mathbb{F}| = \mathsf{poly}(n)$ . Let  $\beta' = \log^{c'} n/2$  for constant c' > c such that  $\beta' > \beta$ . We show NP-hardness of  $\mathsf{GapMWSP}_{\beta'}$  via a reduction from  $\mathsf{GapLabelCover}_{1,\delta}$  for  $\delta = 2/\beta' = 1/\log^{c'} n$ . Since  $\beta' > \beta$ , this shows NP-hardness of  $\mathsf{GapMWSP}_{\beta}$ . Let  $\mathcal{I} = (G = (L, R, E), \Sigma_L, \Sigma_R, \{\pi_e\}_{e \in E})$  be a  $\mathsf{GapLabelCover}_{1,\delta}$  instance. First, by iterative application of Lemma D.3 (at most  $\log |R|$  times), we can assume that |L| > |R|. We construct our  $\mathsf{GapMWSP}_{\beta'}$  instance as follows:

- For each vertex  $v \in L \cup R$  and for each possible label  $\ell \in \Sigma_L \cup \Sigma_R$  for node v, introduce a variable  $x_{v,\ell}$ . Let  $n = |L||\Sigma_L| + |R||\Sigma_R|$  be the number of variables.
- Define the following family of linear constraints over the variables  $x_{v,\ell} \in \mathbb{F}$ :
  - $\forall s \in L : \sum_{\ell \in \Sigma_L} x_{s,\ell} = 1.$
  - $\forall t \in R : \sum_{\ell \in \Sigma_R} x_{t,\ell} = 1.$
  - $\forall (s,t) \in E, \forall \ell \in \Sigma_R : \sum_{r \in \Sigma_L : \pi_{s,t}(r) = \ell} x_{s,r} = x_{t,\ell}.$

{66}------------------------------------------------

Let  $m = |L| + |R| + |E||\Sigma_R|$  be the number of constraints. Define a matrix  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{F}^{m \times n}$  and a target vector  $\mathbf{b} \in \mathbb{F}^m$  corresponding to the above system of linear constraints.

• We set the weight to d = |L| + |R|.

The  $\mathsf{GapMWSP}_{\beta'}$  instance is then the tuple  $\mathcal{I}' = (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{b}, d)$ . To conclude the proof, it suffices to show that there is a one-to-one correspondence between YES instances and NO instances for  $\mathsf{GapLabelCover}_{1,\delta}$  and  $\mathsf{GapMWSP}_{\beta'}$ :

- Low-weight solution if  $\mathcal{I}$  is satisfiable: Suppose the GapLabelCover<sub>1, $\delta$ </sub> instance  $\mathcal{I}$  is satisfiable. Let  $\phi_L, \phi_R$  be the satisfying labeling function. By construction, we can set  $x_{s,\phi_L(s)} = 1$  for all  $s \in L$  and  $x_{t,\phi_R(t)} = 1$  for all  $t \in R$ , and all other  $x_{v,\ell} = 0$  to obtain a satisfying assignment for  $\mathcal{I}'$ . Moreover, there are only |L| + |R| = d nonzero entries in L.
- No low-weight solutions if  $\mathcal{I}$  is not  $\delta$ -satisfiable: Suppose that  $\mathcal{I}$  is not  $\delta$ -satisfiable. Take any solution  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{F}^n$  where  $\mathbf{A}\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{b}$ . The first two constraints in the linear system ensure that for every vertex  $v \in L \cup R$ , there is at least one label  $\ell \in \Sigma_L \cup \Sigma_R$  such that  $x_{v,\ell} \neq 0$ . Therefore, for every vertex v we can define a non-empty set of potential labels  $L_v = {\ell \in \Sigma_L \cup \Sigma_R : x_{v,\ell} \neq 0}$ .

Consider the randomized strategy for the LabelCover problem where every vertex  $v \in L \cup R$  is labeled with a random label  $\ell_v \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} L_v$ . Take any edge  $(s,t) \in E$ , and let  $\ell_t \in \Sigma_R$  be the label assigned to  $t \in R$ . By assumption,  $x_{t,\ell_t} \neq 0$ . By the third set of constraints,  $\sum_{r \in \Sigma_L : \pi_{s,t}(r) = \ell_s} x_{s,r} = x_{t,\ell_t} \neq 0$ . Thus, there must exist some  $r \in \Sigma_L$  such that  $\pi_{s,t}(r) = \ell_s$  and  $x_{s,r} \neq 0$ . In other words,  $r \in L_s$ . Since  $\ell_s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} L_s$ , this means that edge (s,t) is satisfied with probability at least  $1/|L_s|$ . Since every node in L has equal degree, the expected fraction of constraints satisfied by this labeling strategy is

$$\mathbb{E}_{s \in L} \left[ \frac{1}{|L_s|} \right] \ge \frac{1}{\mathbb{E}_{s \in L}[|L_s|]},$$

where the inequality follows from Jensen's inequality. Since  $\mathcal{I}$  is not  $\delta$ -satisfiable, at most a  $\delta$ -fraction of the constraints are satisfiable, so this means that

$$\delta \ge \mathbb{E}_{s \in L} \left[ \frac{1}{|L_s|} \right] \ge \frac{1}{\mathbb{E}_{s \in L}[|L_s|]},$$

or equivalently,  $\mathbb{E}_{s\in L}[|L_s|] \geq 1/\delta$ . This means that there are at least  $|L|/\delta$  variables  $x_{s,\ell}$  where  $s\in |L|$  and  $\ell\in\Sigma_L$  that are nonzero. Since  $|L|\geq |R|$  by assumption, there are at least

$$\frac{1}{\delta}|L| = \frac{1}{2\delta}(|L| + |L|) > \frac{1}{2\delta}(|L| + |R|) = \beta'd$$

elements in  $\mathbf{x}$  that are nonzero. We conclude that if  $\mathcal{I}$  is not  $\delta$ -satisfiable, then there are no solutions to  $\mathcal{I}'$  with weight less than  $\beta'd$ .

Finally, we need to show that there is a Karp-Levin reduction from SAT to GapMWSP $_{\beta}$ . First, we use the fact that the proof of Theorem D.2 from [MR10, DS14] gives a Karp-Levin reduction from SAT to the GapLabelCover $_{1,\delta}$  problem (via the PCP theorem). Moreover, our reduction above gives a Karp-Levin reduction from GapLabelCover $_{1,\delta}$  to GapMWSP $_{\beta'}$  (for any choice of finite field  $\mathbb F$  where  $\log |\mathbb F| = \operatorname{poly}(n)$ ). Composing the two reductions yields a Karp-Levin reduction from SAT to GapMWSP $_{\beta'}$  (and correspondingly, to GapMWSP $_{\beta}$ ) over any finite field  $\mathbb F$  here  $\log |\mathbb F| = \operatorname{poly}(n)$ .

## <span id="page-66-0"></span>E Witness Encryption from Predictable Arguments
